		<date>2006-06-01</date>
		<username>robb_772</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The origin of intelligent Sci-Fi</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The early-fifties saw a huge amount of "flying saucer" pictures storming the US box office, most of which were hastily-made and primarily existed with the humble intention of providing an hour-and-half of cheap fun for film-goers. In 1951, director Robert Wise (best-known for directing 1961's WEST SIDE STORY and 1965's THE SOUND OF MUSIC) and screenwriter Edmund H. North (of 1934's ALL THE KING'S HORSES) created this fantastic film that follows many guidelines of the sub-genre, but digs much deeper than the average "alien invasion" film. The film is not only significantly more advanced in the film-making prowess than similar films from this era, but also in philosophy and social commentary. This film was nominally about an human-like alien and a giant robot, but, in a latent sense, it was about the then-current condition of the human race.Working from author Harry Bates' clever short story, Wise and North have created an imaginative parable on the dehumanizing Red Scare of the late-forties/early-fifties that is neither heavy handed, nor too abstract. I seriously doubt that the commentary on McCarthism will be lost on anyone who views this movie within the proper context, however, the views of the film-makers fortunately refrain from pounding the metaphor into our head (I think nearly everyone can agree McCarthism was unconstitutional, we don't need a movie to convince us of this). Wise and North simply use alien invader Carpenter as a mirror to reflect the hysterical actions of the majority of the public, with as little judgment as possible. The subtle approach not only makes the impact of the film's message even more effective, but it also keeps the picture from dating when viewed outside of it's original context.Further assets include the impressively high production values, which includes a convincing-looking spacecraft, and the imposing robot Gort (played by the very tall Lock Martin). Wise also draws out believable performances from the entire cast, especially handsome Michael Rennie, who tackles his then-unconventional role with intelligence and just the right touch of whimsy. The film also benefits immensely from downplaying the various supernatural occurrences; Wise basically films the picture as a straight drama for the most part, which lends an immeasurable aura of credibility to the fantastical proceedings. Over 55 years later, THE DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL remains a benchmark in the science fiction film-making, and it served as crucial launching pad for many of the intelligent genre films that would follow.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Aggression can never be used to fight peace</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A world without war? It's as unconceivable now as it was then, but thinking about it, it sure would be swell. I'd say this movie is a classic if ever anyone asked me. A classic science fiction film with just the bare minimum of sci-fi elements that focuses more on human stories. Not just by making the space man a lot like us, but with a story about a man from space with a message for this world, who needs to understand the Earth's people and their actions before he can know how to best reach them. Would it be patriotic for us to say we love our planet? We know from living here that too many nations can't live without war, how could a foreigner, from another planet as well, appreciate this fact? Maybe there are things this movie doesn't show us or try to tell us about if this were to realistically happen. But it sure is able to cook up an interesting story to go with an ambitious idea.And right there you understand what the sci-fi genre is, a genre where the conflict inherent in making one is how does being human fit into a story about aliens? So if the alien is more like us, we're more likely to pay attention and buy into it. Science fiction, I'll admit, is not one of my favorite genres. It's nature is generally so advanced that it requires viewers to be on another wavelength to want to be taken to other worlds. But films like this really help us to see the benefits and virtues of this genre, to give a new sense to the quality of dramatization, and give us something completely unknown to try and relate to. Infiltrating our society draws out questions of character and makes us apply human characteristics to something that isn't human, not like we as humans are. And this creates the possibility for a greater understanding of things unknown.The greatest conflict in this story comes from, if this is a film where the main character wants to save Earth by giving us information, if the least we can't do is treat him with respect and trust him just a little, do we feel we deserve to be saved? If you take him at the actions he demonstrates, there's no reason not to trust him. But in this film, characters let hysteria and confusion get the better of them. Which is a very realistic and unfortunate way of portraying it. Though it shows you more of the strengths of the sci-fi genre, in a classic film such as this, you don't find the same sentiment as with war dramas or westerns that have the very same amount of humanity. Well, maybe it has the same idea at heart but the storytelling is focused on technical credibility, that it teaches viewers to be less wrapped up in emotionalism, which gets too sappy at times.So I really like the potential science fiction has to tell a different kind of story. But more often you see that what the genre is mostly comprised of are second-rate stories that are completely preoccupied with the fantasy of foreign things where you have to look for the qualities you have in common. You'll find sometimes these other programs beat you over the head with their message and leave nothing familiar to hold onto. This film tells it's story in a wonderful way that leaves for a lot of meaningful thought afterward. In the end, it all comes down to the question of war - is it necessary. We know why we use war to protect ourselves, but that doesn't mean we should ignore the idea that some societies can live without war and trust peace. That the idea of peace is never suppression of our instinct to protect ourselves. And that we can do just as much good by thinking than by showing aggression. We are humans after all, not animals.As for film quality, this is a movie that I'd like to walk around in. Washington D.C. seems like it used to be a wonderful place of culture and heritage, where you could walk around at night and really see something non-threatening. But then, wasn't all America like that at one point? The film's music score is very simple, and mostly understated, until several key moments where I recognize how many films were influenced by this film just in the music department. I viewed this movie for the first time only recently and this isn't the typical 'something strange is about us' score cues, but instead they suggest something very sinister is underneath the image of a spaceship and an indestructible robot. I guess there's something sinister about everything if you think about it, but I think the film must have consciously had this intention in mind to keep us guessing. For it's time, this film is revolutionary and one of a kind.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Sci-Fi With a Real Message</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Day The Earth Stood Still continues to rank as one of the most popular sci-fi "cult" films more than 50 years after its release, and with good reason: it reduces an important, timeless message for humanity to its essence.This film calls for much suspension of disbelief. For example, only two youthful, incompetent soldiers guard a giant UFO parked in Washington DC. The special effects aren't bad, but at times rank only slightly above the special effects that made Ed Wood infamous. However, it is easy to overlook the "cheese" in this film, thanks to a strong story and fine acting.This film is definitely worth watching because of Michael Rennie's magnificent portrayal of an alien (Klatu) trying to understand human behavior. Almost the entire story is told by Klatu's reactions to what he sees and hears. Only one speech, at the very end, articulates the film's message in words, and this is probably a good thing, lest even this valid message begin to sound like preaching.The music score, featuring one of the earliest uses of the spooky-sounding Theremin (made famous by the Star Trek theme), adds an essential character to the film. Without that music, the film's pacing would have been too slow. Combined with the music, however, the pacing is almost perfect.The special edition DVD is rather sparse on features, but they are some of the finest features I've seen on DVD. The 70-minute documentary and audio commentary with the original filmmakers actually focuses on how the film was made, rather than telling what a great guy the assistant grip was. Anyone interested in how films are actually made will thoroughly enjoy these special features, and learn a lot about film-making.Watching the film again in 2005, I'm made conscious of the fact that our political (and business) leaders still haven't gotten the message, while almost every other segment of society has evolved beyond medieval notions that violence is the answer to every problem. If Klatu landed in Washington DC today, I suspect a high-ranking member of our current government would shoot him first and probably never ask questions.And that is precisely why The Day The Earth Stood Still's message is still important today.8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>When less was so much more</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Limited resources force ingenuity from the creative artist often resulting in fine work. In the fifties, Robert Wise's work promised great things. His lean, sharp, economic style was used to great effect particularly in the superb "The Set-Up" and the now classic "Day The Earth Stood Still".By the mid sixties Hollywood lavished huge budgets on him which resulted in flabby, overblown bores such as "Sand Pebbles", "Star" and the saccharine drenched "Sound of Music". It's hard to believe that the same man was behind all these projects."The Day the Earth Stood Still" is a lesson in film making in all its aspects. The special effects often laughable in fifties sci-fi movies are simple yet startlingly credible. Scenes are shot with obvious care to camera angle and are enhanced by the just slightly menacing Bernard Herrmann score.The actors are well cast all turning in fine performances. There is a touch of the alien in Michael Rennie's bony, statuesque physique making him just right as Klaatu. Patricia Neal exudes both beauty and intelligence and thankfully child actor Billy Gray avoids the pitfalls that others of his ilk are so often prone to, with a refreshing naturalness in his acting.While the message tends to be labored, its as timely as it was in the early fifties. Not surprisingly this one made in IMDb's top 250.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Timeless Scifi Classic.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Nostalgia of days past when television was in its monochromatic infancy that I recollect my very first experience of this movie and the shroud of fear that reigned on me thereafter. Such is the innocence of childhood that my parents made me promise I never watched such movies until the right coming of age.Watching it for the second time years later it left an impression like no other such that today I own it on Blu-ray, forming part of a small collection of timeless classics of which I never tire. The Day the Earth Stood Still epitomises the true genre of science fiction where benign extraterrestrial intelligence as the centre point supplants the then notion of alien life forms with hostile intent.This film thrives on an excellent script brought to life through contrived direction and carefully chosen cast. There is little censure impinging upon it other than certain limitations on the special effects, but even so moments like the landing of the alien craft truly elevates it beyond latter efforts in other productions. Although in essence the story unfolds with the arrival of an emissary travelling the endless boundaries of the universe and landing in Washington to warn the leaders of all nations of impending doom should their warring differences threaten intergalactic peace and security, the focal point of the movie remains the bond that develops between Klatu and Bobby, whose innocence is never defaced by the erudite answers given him by the enigmatic stranger to questions posed.The climax finally adds to the paranoia of humanity upon request of proof born of scientific curiosity when the electricity throughout the planet is neutralised, enough to raise panic but benign not to cause major ruin. It is the simplicity with which this movie delivers a clear message that no remake is deserving of higher praise.This film is manifold boundless in every aspect and will never be matched through any contemporary standard. It is a masterpiece in its own right and place in time, and a revisit of the kind can only transcend through retrospection.Scott Derrickson and David Scarpa would have managed a worthy reproduction had they had the vision of Peter Jackson, Fran Walsh and Philippa Boyens.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>If ever we needed Klaatu, its now in 2001!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A strange film to have attained such classic status, one that grows with each passing decade. It is in the sum of its parts that it delivers such power, rather than any one contributing factor. Consider if you will: There is virtually no action throughout its 92 minute runtime, What fx there ARE, would hardly stir one to marvel at their awesome reality! It is so very dated in terms of visuals and characters. The script though literate, does not move one automatically to magnanimous praise. With the exception of Michael Rennie, himself barely "A" list at the time, there is no reason to watch the film for its star-power....so what IS it about it this movie that has drawn about it such hushed legend?The answer probably lies in the statement 'different things for different people' but THE DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL absolutely COMMANDS respect and the intimate attention of anyone who ever thought "What is happening to humanity?...Where Are WE GOING?..or more importantly.."Can we get back?""I see several aspects to this most thought-provoking screen gem that I at least, believe are part of the weave. Michael Rennie as Klaatu put forward such earnest compassion and dignity in the role of an extra-terrestrial, yet global saviour (who came to warn, not to judge), the Christ allegory was obvious from the outset. Jesus promised he would return - he made no mention of checking back into his stable in Bethlehem. He came the FIRST time with a request to follow his lead or else to expect judgment from a higher source. He provided miracles that others might witness...Klaatu shuts down the world's power. As before, there were those who listened and those who would condemn. Christ was crucified for his efforts - Klaatu, shot. If EITHER return this century what difference would it likely make? We have learned NOTHING!The other superlative role in this film is that played by veteran Sam Jaffe. As Professor Barnhardt leading US scientist (THE NASA head of his day) he comes to realise the truth of Klaatu's origins. He may be likened to Lucius, the doubting Centurian. His performance is at once touching and respectful.The giant robot Gort whose unimaginable power is always felt but not gratuitously displayed, is a key part also of THE DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL. As menacing and impenetrable a figure as anything ever put on screen, Gort represents the ultimate peacekeeper...the umpire you REALLY wouldn't want to mess with! It must also be remembered that the film was directed by Robert Wise who himself had dignity and vision. The director's influence is clearly a large part of the finished product and with this, Wise has carved for himself, a niche of immortality.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Get beyond the technology, and this film still works!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have several younger friends, who...unfortunately...can't get beyond their heritage. THEIR heritage is Star Wars. If it doesn't have that "Star Wars" look, they just don't get it. Well, if you're like that, this film is going to be tremendously disappointing. The Day the Earth Stood Still is THE cautionary tale of those of us who grew up under fear of the Atomic Bomb (that's right, the baby-boomers). It only has a nodding acquaintance to Science Fiction, and only about ten minutes of actual special effects footage, mostly at the beginning and the end. Yet that ten minutes or so, is used to GREAT effect. When Gort comes alive, the hairs on the back of my neck STILL stand up; the inside shot of the spaceship is truely "otherwordly". And Michael Rennie absolutely OWNS his character. I simply can't imagine anyone else playing Klaatu. And all of it in "glorious black and white."!!! Sandwish between the two Sci-Fi slices, is the meat of the story. It doesn't picture we humans of the 1950's in quite the same light as "Happy Days," does it? We are seen as fearful, grasping and amazingly lacking in character. Only one woman and one boy stand out...and neither of them are perfect, either. Why does Klaatu even bother to TRY to save us? I think that this is the reason virtually everyone I know either loves or hates this film. It forces us to face some of the worst of our own nature, and it does it with a rough hand on the shoulder. It doesn't pussyfoot around when it says, "shape up or be destroyed." If you want to see a classic "Morality Play" put against a Sci Fi background, with great leading roles...then take in this movie. But if you can't take it with the spirit of those scary times, you simply won't get it! The 50's was like no other era in recent American History. If you don't understand that context, most of the ground-breaking movies of that era, won't make sense to you. But if you come to grips with the paranoia and commercialism of the times, then it'll start to all fit. A classic!!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Good Aliens For a Change</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Perhaps one of the best science fiction films to emerge out of the 50's, The Day the Earth Stood Still is a fine motion picture on so many different levels. It is a wonderful story of an alien that comes to our planet with Earth's interest at the very heart of his business. It is refreshing to see a film from the 50's that has a good alien, one that wants to help and cares for humanity, rather than one that wishes to destroy and use the people and resources of the planet selfishly. The lead is capably filled by Michael Rennie who brings an understanding and innocence to his role few actors could have pulled off. Rennie is the archetypal Christ figure bringing mankind truth, only to be killed for his goodness. Just like Christ, Rennie's character rises from death and ascends to the heavens. Philosophically, the film is a masterful look at human nature both good and evil. The story shows this alien, alike in form, wander the streets of Washington D.C. seeing the progress of man and the impediments to his further progress. Patricia Neal excels in her role as a mother with a son that befriends this stranger. The rest of the cast is also very good. Look for Frances Bavier(Aunt Bea) in a small role. Robert Wise, the director, whether intentionally or not, created a great film that not only tells a very convincing story of what our planet would be like if entertaining an alien presence, but also a telling allegory looking ahead to man's hope and future.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>This is one of the science fiction films that George Lucas would like to have a part of his trilogy. It does not require much to keep our attention, it does not have a mass universal terminology which excludes entire audiences, and its intentions are entirely clear. Instead, without a huge handful of stars, Robert Wise directs at a slow pace which surprisingly enhances the film.The tides change at eventual rate and the black and white cinematography does not allow intrusion into an earlier age with different beliefs. The music haunts, and it poignantly reveals, with little effort, the sometimes damning and tragic sides of human nature.The characters of the film are not revealed in huge depths. Visitation to earth for Klaatu was merely a day trip, and with little effort, he delivers his word as what seems like a Christ type figure. The question remains, as for the audiences of its time, our time and even the characters, if we are to listen. This is like the "2001: A Space Odyssey" of the 1950s. While no one can doubt the superiority of Stanley Kubrick's vision over the originally humble black and white grand daddy of all sci-fi, it delivers for the thinking members of society all that is need to be said, more successfully than the other. Does it still work now, with all its powerful 1950s oriented symbolism? Does it remain a film for the modern audiences? The ingenuity of "The Day the Earth Stood Still" as a film for all the ages, need not be questioned.Klaatu is welcome to pay us a visit. For real. Rating: 8/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>top notch sci-fi</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>From the moment the viewer hears Bernard Herrmann's classic use of the theremin for the film's opening music set against the image of a wild galaxy, you know you're in for a treat. The movie continues this sense of awe and excitement through its subsequent use of fantastic special effects (set in the daytime, which makes the effects unnoticeably easy on the eyes)to set up the movie's main premise. The film's scenes move with a lightingly quick pace, though, and so the viewer is never allowed the chance for him/her to be bored with what turns out to be a film more concerned with morale than intergalactic carnage. The acting is appropriately right on in its earnestness, while the script always lends the feel of the film building up to its tremendous climax. It is here where the emotional wallop is felt, and Klaatu's ultimate intentions are brought forth. Nice touches throughout the movie include the exchange between the little boy and Klaatu concerning war, and the explanation of how and why Klaatu looks so human-like. It's anti-nuclear weapons message was very impactful back in the fifties, but the movie also works just as well now, in the late 90's. I guess this is why great science fiction movies such as this are great in the first place.</fullReview>

		<date>1999-06-03</date>
		<username>Donald J. Lamb</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Great Sci-Fi Aims to Enlighten But Essentially Entertains...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Many fans of this classic film from the early 1950's look upon it as a Christ-like message picture with Klaatu, the Martian, delivering a message of possible armageddon or else. They have a point because it does more or less comment on the Cold War atmosphere and paranoia of America at the time. I look at it more as simple entertainment, an intelligent movie with cheesy effects and good performances.THE DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL has remained timeless and watchable because we don't see many effects, which consist mostly of the kitchen sink spaceship and a goofy-looking foam alien bodyguard made of "some of the toughest substance ever seen." Director Robert Wise shoots the film in glossy black and white, which adds an element of slight suspense and originality allowing you to overlook the silly prosthetics.Klaatu hooks up with Albert Einstein-like Professor Barnhardt and solve some celestial physics together. They must also conjure up a way to lower the chances of atomic annihilation amongst the rest of the world's "great" minds. The military is out for blood looking for the escaped alien and a page of WAR OF THE WORLDS has obviously been borrowed. Radio reports and TV broadcasters emulate doom over the airwaves sounding like Orson Welles in 1938. Modern day sci-fi like INDEPENDENCE DAY has borrowed from this with the military presence and sense of fright over the appearance of the visitors from outer space. Thankfully, it does not go over the top with ridiculous sci-fi jargon, which destroys the timelessness of so many inferior extra-terrestrial films.I felt like watching ED WOOD afterwards. This is the picture Edward D. Wood, Jr. probably dreamt of making. Tim Burton pays homage to DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL in MARS ATTACKS! with its dish-like saucers and stand off at the Capitol in Washington, D.C. Don't dismiss the message of peace which is draped all over the movie. I just felt it was more a science fiction story meant to give us a fluffy cinematic experience and I'm sure the picture was innovative in 1951.Listen for yet another fine Bernard Hermann score which has a familiar ghoulish sound evident in most sci-fi flicks of the era. He takes it a step further of course and provides a nice backdrop of dramatically stirring music. Robert Wise is obviously a man of many tastes and talents, directing pictures like WEST SIDE STORY, THE HAUNTING, and even the original STAR TREK movie (yuk!). This is without a doubt an American classic and has stood the test time.RATING: 8 of 10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Klaatu barada nikto!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While I'm not a huge sci-fi fan, I watched this classic for the first time last weekend and thoroughly enjoyed it! Michael Rennie stars as Klaatu, a tall, dignified alien who travels 250 million miles from an unnamed planet to Washington, D.C. He is accompanied by Gort (Lock Martin), a gigantic robot that can destroy weapons with a powerful laser beam. Klaatu takes a room in a boarding house, where he meets widow Helen Benson (Patricia Neal) and her young son Bobby (Billy Gray). (Incidentally, Gray would later play Bud Anderson in the hit TV series "Father Knows Best.") In summary, Klaatu warns the people on earth that they must either pursue peace or face obliteration. For me, everything about this movie meshes very well -- the intelligent plot, the superb cast, the eerie soundtrack, and the modest special effects. Trivia fans will note the obvious parallels between Klaatu and Jesus, e.g. Mr. Carpenter is Klaatu's alias, he prophesies destruction on earth, he dies and later comes back to life. The only thing I wish the screenwriters had changed would be to include Bobby in the final scene -- especially considering that he and Klaatu become good friends during the alien's stay in Washington. Other than that, "The Day the Earth Stood Still" is a sci-fi masterpiece that just gets better with age! Yes, technology has changed, but the film's message remains highly relevant to this day.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>One of the Top 10-1950's Sci-Fi Classics</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I first saw this on NBC's "Saturday Night at the Movies". I never saw it on a big screen. The robot, "Gort" was not as scary on a 21" TV set, but when his "laser eye" opened it gave me chills and it still does. Remarkably simple special effects by today's standards yet highly effective. That "creepy" classic music. Gaunt and tall (6'4") Michael Rennie looks like an alien. Sam Jaffe is the perfect "Einstein-like" eccentric scientist. Patricia Neal, Billy Gray and Hugh Marlowe are credible as the supporting cast in the most unusual sci-fi film ever made. The studio "sold" it as a "monster" movie, which it never was. The robot carries a blond-haired bimbo (in a sultry red dress) in its arms on the lobby cards. Patricia was not blond and did not wear a "red" dress, though it's hard to tell in a b/w movie. It's one of the most thoughtful fantasy films ever made, although not perfect. The "speeded-up" film in the panic scenes looks very fake. The Army is portrayed as brainless and heartless. Poor Gort had wardrobe issues: You can see his air holes and zipper. Even the support "wires" as he carries Patricia back to the spaceship. The Earth doesn't really "stand still". The aliens just neutralize most electricity. That's not very scary, or is it? The movie's "message" is still relevant after 60 years. The people of Earth must learn to live in peace or someday we will all be blasted off the Earth. Whether we will kill ourselves off or are vaporized by alien robots like "Gort" still remains to be seen. A note to "Fox" and everyone else: A movie does not have to remade just because it's in black and white.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2011-08-18</date>
		<username>dimplet</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>60 years on, it improves with age</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It has been 60 years and one month since the debut of "The Day the Earth Stood Still." Rather than the movie looking out of date, the fact that the crude 1951 technology looks so antiquated today helps make the point of the movie: humanity's conceit looks childish in the larger cosmic perspective. Multiply the way 1951 technology looks to us in 2011 times 100 or 1,000 and you begin to get some idea how we must look to an advanced galactic civilization. When this movie was released, televisions were just appearing in American homes, the transistor had been invented four years before and was not in any consumer products, jet planes were limited to the military, and the atomic bomb was marking its sixth birthday. The first satellite was still six years in the future. Man travel to the moon? Maybe in the 21st century.Yes, we've made enormous technological progress in 60 years, and now nearly everyone has a cell phone, or a laptop or an HD TV! How puny we would seem next to a civilization with thousands or even millions of years of scientific progress, instead of our mere 500. And then there were the rash of UFO sightings, including headlines across the nation of a crashed UFO recovered in Roswell, New Mexico in 1947, based on a military press release quickly retracted. What if a UFO did land, say, in Washington, D.C.? People couldn't help but wonder. Bosley Crowther in his New York Times review of Sept. 19, 1951 was not impressed, seeming to prefer the thrills and action of H.G. Wells' War of the Worlds:"It is comforting, of course, to have it made plain that our planetary neighbors are much wiser and more peaceful than are we, but this makes for a tepid entertainment in what is anomalously labeled the science- fiction field."Crowther, it seems, did not want to face the issues head on, and did not understand the essence of modern science fiction, which is less about technology than about questions of human nature, society and even spirituality, set in some science fiction future. In other words, it is supposed to make you think outside the box of your little reality.Sadly, humanity seems to have made little progress in the social or spiritual realm over the past 60 years. For awhile, it seemed we were learning to cooperate among nations to address our problems, but now it seems more like things are flying apart. Could we call a meeting of all heads of state to meet with a visitor from another planet today? Well, there are some that seem preoccupied with civil wars at the moment. And there are others who are corrupt, or who are hopelessly mediocre and incompetent. With the perspective of 60 years, it is clear why UFOs would not want to land and meet our leaders then, and why they still would not, today. Humanity must seem like so many childish fools for being unable to face facts and cooperate to solve our imminent and urgent problems. The Day the Earth Stood Still is a classic, but it got some basic science wrong. That an advanced space ship would take five months to travel 250 million miles is pretty pathetic. That's about the distance to Mars. (The next Mars rover, Curiosity, will take 255 days to travel the 354 million miles to Mars at a leisurely 58,000 mph, or 16 miles per second.)Yet in the opening, we see vistas that seem to represent the view from the spaceship as it travels to Earth, and these include a view from a distance of our galaxy, which is far more than 250 million miles. It would have been more realistic to talk of billions or trillions of miles, or of light years, but for a time when we had not yet even sent a rocket into space, this might have been too hard to grasp.The interior of the spaceship is pretty cool. (I'm not sure what I would do in there for five months; where's the bed and kitchen and AV console? Did Klaatu play gin rummy with Gort for the entire trip? Barbarella's shag-carpeted RV spaceship looks like more fun.) Reportedly, it was designed by Frank Lloyd Wright. The Bernard Herrmann score also holds up surprisingly well. Some people gripe that Hugh Marlowe's performance is stiff. But his character was an insurance salesman; he's a conservative with little imagination. I've seen plenty of people like this over the years, and Marlowe got it right. The aliens had the last laugh. Flying saucers were observed flying in formation over Washington, D.C. the next year, on radar and in photographs, apparently in a case of reality imitating art. It got front page headlines in the Washington Post. Wikipedia has an article, "1952 Washington D.C. UFO incident." There were numerous other sightings around the nation in 1952, and reports of a possible "shoot down" order if the UFOs did not obey orders to land. Yeah, right. One part of the movie does look especially quaint today, and that is the threat to destroy Earth if we did not learn to live together in peace. We don't need some outsiders threatening us; we are quite capable of destroying our planet all by ourselves through global warming. Our planet seems to have a built in self-destruct mechanism if we fail to learn to cooperate to solve our problems. This time, I'm afraid, simply repeating "Klaatu barada nikto" won't cut it, though it's worth a try.Trivia: Klaatu takes the name John Carpenter when he leaves the hospital, an ironic choice in light of the story, with the initials JC matching the founder of Christianity, who was a carpenter. Then, 33 years later, another movie about a visitor from space, Starman, was directed by John Carpenter.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>One of my favorite science fiction films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When the alien messenger Klaatu lands his flying saucer in Washington DC, he is wounded by a nervous soldier and imprisoned in a hospital by the military. When he escapes, he seeks out a boarding house so that he can meet the people of this planet. He befriends a small boy and a peace loving professor, and is hunted by the authorities. When governments refuse to listen to his message, he gives an impressive demonstration of his power and leaves a warning for the people of Earth.The Day the Earth Stood Still is a wonderful and simple tale, skilfully and artistically put on the big screen by director Robert Wise. It is beautifully filmed, has a great script that emphasizes the relationships between the characters in a very real way despite its fantastic subject, and is absolutely perfectly paced. Michael Rennie has a quiet authority and charisma in his role as Klaatu that makes him an excellent choice for the part, and I was also impressed by Sam Jaffe as the professor.The movie has a nice, if trite, message of peace but unlike many other science fiction movies, accents this with a lack of violent action by the aliens, even if the threat of violence remains. The aliens here are not seen as evil conquerors and destroyers-a nice change.Overall, a great movie that has held up through the decades and is still a great story today.</fullReview>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Day the Earth Stood Still (1951)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After revisiting this film just now, I have one word to say: Powerful. TDTESS is one of the greatest Cold War allegories ever created, but it really does transcend the time period it was created in and becomes simply a powerful film about the irrational fears, prejudices and jealousies of the human race, and the consequences of our behavior.A spaceship is detected landing in Washington D.C., setting off mass panic all around the world, and prompting heavy military response. Upon arrival, two figures are seen exiting the spacecraft: Klaatu, a mysterious visitor who looks remarkably human, and Gort, an advanced and dangerous robot. As the world watches the events unfolding before them on television and radio, Klaatu desperately seeks out an audience composed of representatives from all the nations and cultures of the world in order to give them an important message, a message that concerns the continuation of all life on planet Earth.When you watch this film you really have to be aware of the Cold War mentality of the time period, but the film works remarkably well even if you place it in the context of today's society. This is apparently why they have decided to create a remake, in theaters this December. Based on the trailer, I'm unsure if the spirit of this film will remain. I hope so, because this is an important film about issues concerning all of humanity. Perhaps they can find a new, more contemporary issue on which to draw inspiration for the remake.TDTESS constantly reminds us of the achievements made by humanity, including some very touching scenes involving the national landmarks and memorials in the Washington D.C. area, while also making a statement about the fragility of our society. Its themes are universal, including cultural intolerance, aggression, apathy, greed, corruption, and innocence. It's an extremely intelligent film that sustains interest, and all the way until the end it remains a compelling and interesting human study.10/10, a masterpiece.</fullReview>

		<username>Boba_Fett1138</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>It still is one of the best alien 'invasion' movies ever made.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is so totally refreshing different than its fellow genre movies from the '50's! Everyone has a certain images in their mind about '50's science-fiction movies, with flying saucers of which you can see the wires, aliens with bad make-up effects and simple plots. The type of Ed Wood movies. "The Day the Earth Stood Still" is a much, much different movie fortunately!It's not a movie that tries to impress, it's instead more of a movie that tries to provoke and at all time remain as realistic as possible. The movie its message is clear and (unfortunately) it still applies today, just as much as it did over 50 years ago.Normally these type of movies from this time period would be about aliens causing mayhem and destruction, by trying to take over or simply destroy the Earth. This movie does begin as one of those sort of movies but soon starts to take a positive turn, when it starts to totally focus on its script and story, rather than any action or over-the-top science-fiction moments. It's a clever and provoking movie. Everything happening in this movie happens with a reason.The way the alien visits Earth and tries to discover things and places is done in a great way. It's a reflecting and objective view of a neutral person. It's really a movie that holds up a mirror to the world and therefor works great and effective as a social commentary, that still applies today.The movie has some great convincing special effects (especially for '50's standards of course) that are still acceptable by today standards. The movie is not as cheap looking as most of the genre movies from the same time period, though I imaging this movie had just such an high budget as any other. So also real big kudos to director Robert Wise who brought it all so very well and convincing to the screen. Robert Wise would later make also such science-fiction classics as "The Andromeda Strain" and "Star Trek: The Motion Picture". Another thing that makes this movie distinct itself from other is the classic Bernard Herrmann score.The main character is greatly played by the unknown Michael Rennie . He really has the right looks of an alien in human form. Also surprising how important and strong the female character played by Patricia Neal in this movie is. The always great Sam Jaffe also plays a good, though small role in the movie. But the real classic and most memorable character of the movie is the robot Gort, played by the very tall Lock Martin. Even though it's not a character that talks or even movies a lot, it's still one that impresses very much, just by its looks and actions. 	<review>
		<date>2007-04-13</date>
		<username>ackstasis</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>"Your choice is simple. Join us and live in peace, or pursue your present course and face obliteration."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1951, in the aftermath of World War II and at the beginning of the Cold War, there existed considerable cultural fears related to violence, politics and even possible global annihilation if the trend towards human war and conflict continued. With the further development of atomic weapons, and the beginning of the Space Age just six short years away, these fears were beginning to intensify. Many science-fiction films of the 1950s simply exploited this paranoia, using alien invasions as an allegory to a Soviet invasion (the excellent 'Invasion of the Body Snatchers (1956)' being the most accomplished example). However, Robert Wise' science-fiction classic, 'The Day the Earth Stood Still,' is very much a cautionary tale, challenging the audience to consider what will become of humanity if we continue our current course, and pleading for a change in our destructive ways.The film gets straight into the action. Behind the opening credits, we briefly observe the approaching spacecraft's journey across the Universe, into the Milky Way, and towards a tiny planet Earth. The spacecraft steeps into our atmosphere, the clouds disperse and we glimpse the familiar open ocean. Radio and television stations announce the mysterious UFO's presence to the world. It travels at supersonic speed ? 4000 miles per hour ? and nobody can identify exactly what it is (to add another layer of reality, some of the era's most famous broadcast journalists were hired to provide cameos as themselves ? including Gabriel Heatter, H.V. Kaltenborn and Drew Pearson). Miraculously, the spacecraft slows above Washington D.C., and, to the horror of onlookers, lands dramatically on the Ellipse at President's Park.A curious crowd begins to gather around the mysterious craft; squadrons of heavily-armed army personnel cautiously surround it. Along the spacecraft's perfectly smooth body, an entrance opens and a ramp is lowered to the grass. A spaceman, dressed in a shiny, silver suit and accompanied by a massive impenetrable robot named Gort, steps out into the view of the stunned crowd. His first words are friendly and reassuring: "we have come to visit you in peace and with goodwill." Nevertheless, at the first sudden movement, a soldier's pistol is fired and the spaceman is struck. Already, Man is demonstrating his uncanny ability for destruction.As this mysterious spaceman, named Klaatu (Michael Rennie), reveals his intentions of saving the planet from potential obliteration, he is saddened to hear of the insurmountable gulfs between countries that hamper his plans. Embroiled in all their petty human politics, the countries of the world are simply unable to come to a mutual understanding, thus practically sealing their own demise. Klaatu quite easily flees from the detention of the hospital, assumes a new identity (as a Mr. Carpenter) and hires a room in a household full of with conspiracy theorists. It is here that he comes to know Helen Benson (Patricia Neal) and her son Bobby (Billy Gray).It is the latter who ? not knowing Klaatu's true identity ? suggests that he visit Prof. Jacob Barnhardt (Sam Jaffe), purportedly one of the most intelligent men in the world. There is a familiar way in which Klaatu smiles at Barnhardt's complex mathematical calculations on the blackboard, as one would smile at a child learning addition and subtraction. It reminds us that, even with all our technological advancement, we are still but children in the eyes of the Universe. Nevertheless, Klaatu decides that, if anything is to be done to save the fate of the planet, it must take place through men whose judgment is not clouded by government policies and petty prejudices. He must approach the scientists of the world, the scholars of art, philosophy and culture; those whose only concern is the search for knowledge and happiness. It is not the bickering governments who will play any large part in this tale ? the President of the United States doesn't even earn himself an appearance ? but the uncorrupted hearts and minds of the rational thinkers.'The Day The Earth Stood Still' was adapted by Edmund H. North from Harry Bates' short story 'Farewell to the Master.' In the original story's shock ending, it is revealed that, contrary to initial belief, it is the robot Gort (named Gnut in the story) who is the master, and Klaatu who is the servant. This ending is not clearly addressed in the film itself ? though Klaatu does declare that, in order to maintain universal peace, they created a race of robots and, in matters of aggression, gave them absolute power of everyone, even over their creators. Despite this, Klaatu is inarguably given the role of a Messiah, most prominently by featuring a resurrection scene, and also by his choice of alias ? Jesus himself was a carpenter by trade.Countless later science-fiction films owe a lot to 'The Day The Earth Stood Still.' Roland Emmerich's 1996 blockbuster 'Independence Day' borrowed heavily from it, and acknowledges this by briefly featuring a clip from the film itself. The hysteria and confusion arising from the sudden loss of electricity and transport no doubt inspired Steven Spielberg for his 2005 remake of 'The War of the Worlds.' Most significantly, this film strongly reflects many of the cultural fears inherent in that era. One elderly female conspiracy theorist declares: "if you want my opinion, he (Klaatu) comes from right here on Earth. And you know where I mean." Yes, we immediately do. This paranoia between the two feuding powers is explicitly shown to be the main reason why Klaatu's initial peace proposal was a failure, as neither was able to reach an acceptable peace compromise by which the visitor's proposition could be heard. Mankind's primitive tendency towards violence, throughout the film, threatens to destroy any chance of its continuing existence, though the ending is deliberately left very open ending, as if the ultimate choice is left to the viewers themselves.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Excellent sci-fi film... the one true flaw is the heavy-handed, preachy presentation of its moral</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had heard about this film a number of times before I got to watch it. It's been lauded as one of the best science fiction movies ever, and there are numerous references to it in other movies and on TV. I definitely understand its popularity now. The plot is interesting and develops nicely throughout. The pacing is pretty much solid all the way, I was never bored. The effects hold up remarkably well, and are seldom anything less than completely convincing. The writing is quite good, with credible characters and events(considering the genre, obviously); it did, however, stand out how one character seems to disappear for the final act with no explanation. The acting is fine; it doesn't soar to Oscar-winning heights(nor does it drop to the point where it's embarrassing), but it worked well for the film. I thought that the event which the title refers to held a great point, and worked well as satire and social commentary. The music has the disadvantage of being produced at a time where music for sci-fi alien/UFO stuff was what by any standard today is considered corny; this piece deserves a much more fitting score, but if you can overlook that(which I had little trouble doing), it's still a highly enjoyable movie. The film plays much more seriously than many sci-fi films(particularly of the period, and for dealing with UFOs), and works really well as such. The one thing that brings down the overall quality is the way the moral of the story is presented... I realize that movies did occasionally spoon-feed their points around this time(Scarface of '32 being a prime example thereof, although this film certainly isn't as bad as that), but it's difficult to listen to the point being presented, valid as it arguably may be, when it's being preached to us. Still, this is a fine piece of science fiction, and should be experienced by any and all fans of such. I recommend this to any fan of the people involved in making it as well as the genre of sci-fi. 8/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>skallisjr</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Like Wine, Age Makes It Better</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this when it first came out, as an early teenager. I had previously read "Farewell To the Master," the story the screenplay was based upon, which had a solider jolt to it than the film. However, for younger viewers, it's better to understand the historical context.When the film was made, we had no space program: a few rocket probes were being shot up to what later would be called "satellite height," but the concept of an artificial satellite would be unknown to the public for a few years. There were "big" rockets for atmospheric research, but many of them were refurbished V-2 missiles. There was some ferment in the public mind, and nuclear weapons were considered good things to have (there were all sorts of businesses that put "atomic" in their names, for example). Films like Destination Moon whetted the public's appetite for science fiction (resulting in clunkers like Rocketship X-M and The Man Fom Planet X), and this one fell right into this environment.The story in the film was simple: alien envoy comes to Earth to warn humans not to export nuclear weaponry to space or get zapped. He's wounded, hospitalized, and then works undercover until he was able to contact a leading scientist, Professor Barnhardt. He understands that he has to communicate with an international scientific community, and he delivers his message.An interesting point, tangentially a possible spoiler. Klaatu breaks into Professor Barnhardt's study, and corrects some of his math. When the professor asks him how he knows that his corrections are valid, he explains that it helped him travel some 250 million miles to reach the Earth. Now given the dimensions of the Solar System, this suggests strongly that the spacecraft came from a nearby planet, almost certainly Mars. Now had Klaatu merely said, "I'm from Mars," the impact on the audience would have been laughter: the idea of Men From Mars had been a cultural joke for many years before, possibly a byproduct of the radio "War Of the Worlds" scare.Now Klaatu's solution for World Peace (an incorruptible robotic police force) was very simplistic, and I could envision such mechanisms getting hacked, but that's almost another matter (a 1947 Buck Rogers radio show had a robotic civilization where Buck and company were being held prisoner until they were able to sneak in and reprogram the new head robot, so the concept would not have been that far-fetched). But it was a good way to give the film a quick ending that was consistent with Klaatu's perspective.I've enjoyed the film, and have it in my library. It's a good repeat view.</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2005-01-26</date>
		<username>Lechuguilla</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>1950's Time Capsule</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After WWII and the invention of the atomic bomb, people in the 50's were looking for strong leaders who could command moral authority. Enter General Eisenhower, who would become a two-term U.S. President; "Father Knows Best", with its stern but loving father figure; and the iron-fisted biblical epic: "The Ten Commandments".Enter, too, "The Day The Earth Stood Still", a 1951 sci-fi classic soaked in images of authority ... the police, the military, and the tall, stern, Moses-like figure from another world, Klaatu, who lectures us on the folly of nuclear war. It is, I believe, this pacifist message emanating from strength that explains the film's enduring popularity.Noble as the message is, what about its cinematic vehicle? Visually and musically, the film is appropriately frigid and forbidding. Leo Tover's noirish B&amp;W lighting and unobtrusive camera work, combined with Bernard Herrmann's score and the eerie theramin sounds all work in concert to convey a mood of Orwellian severity and other worldly coldness.But the script is disappointing. This is a very talky film, which dilutes its effectiveness as sci-fi. The dialogue seems stodgy, canned, uninspired. Example: "attention zone 5, attention zone 5, yellow cab moving north ... man and woman in backseat; get license number and report ... deploy all units according to Plan B immediately" (well, at least it was not Plan 9...).This pedestrian script could have been borrowed from most any cops and robbers flick of the 40's. On the other hand, I guess I can forgive the script's moratorium on humor, given the seriousness of the message.The film's science is very dated, thus requiring further tolerance. Klaatu to scientists: "The universe grows smaller every day"; no, actually the physical universe is expanding. "Venus and Mars ... are the only two planets capable of sustaining life as we know it"; no, not with their temperature extremes and chemical composition.And the special effects are curiously minimal in the same era that produced the beautifully weird gliding machines from Mars in "War Of The Worlds" (1953), and the lurking terror in "Forbidden Planet" (1956).One solid accomplishment of this film is its accurate portrayal of society in the early 1950's: the old cars, men's formal attire (especially those hats), interior decor, antiquated TV sets, and of course the confidence in institutional authority.In summary then, the movie, for me, functions less as a credible sci-fi vehicle than as a fascinating socio-political commentary on American life in the early 1950's.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2016-07-05</date>
		<username>Leofwine_draca</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Movie with a message</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A surprisingly intelligent sci-fi flick from the '50s, made before the craze for murderous cannibalistic aliens and monsters took over. What could easily have turned into another mindless 'alien invasion' flick turns out to be more interested in the story's moral, rather than extensive scenes of mass destruction as the aliens destroy Earth. Rightly regarded as a classic within the genre, this pulls the correct strings in all aspects concerned. The score is both spooky and exciting, thanks to Bernard Herrman's work (the man also did PSYCHO). The production values are all good too, and the film is refreshingly free of military and scientific jargon which would have dated it and therefore made it inaccessible to the modern audience.The acting is all top notch, from the realistic boy actor, Billy Grey, who becomes Klaatu's friend, to the surprisingly strong heroine who for once isn't forced around by the men in the film; in one good scene she tells her husband-to-be where to go after he ignores her pleas of rationality and decides to act irresponsibly ("The rest of the world doesn't matter!"). There is even a crusty professor who isn't irritating in the slightest. Obviously, though, it's Michael Rennie who carries the film in his pivotal role, and we see things from his point of view. Rennie is excellent as the spaceman, kind and compassionate, and yet with a hard edge which also shows itself occasionally.The special effects are used sparingly and therefore are all the better for it. While the flying UFO isn't too realistic, the robot, Gort, is very impressive as he fires lasers and destroys weapons. Apart from an exciting cab chase at the end of the film with the army closing in, there is relatively little action, the film is more concerned with theorising and discussion. It also paints a very accurate portrayal of mankind, a believable, realistic and frightening one.There is just so much to watch this film for, whether it be the classic images of the indestructible robot coming out of the UFO, the oft-quoted dialogue ("Klaatu verata nikto!"), the religious allegory (with Rennie as Christ) or the film's message (let's stop fighting and get rid of our atomic weaponry), still applicable to this day. THE DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL richly deserves the status that it has acquired. For those with taste...</fullReview>

		<date>2014-01-22</date>
		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>So There We Have It. Deterrence Works.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In science fiction, especially in the cinema, aliens visiting Earth tend to fall into two categories, the Hostile Alien, as in "Signs", "Independence Day" or the various versions of "The War of the Worlds", and the Friendly Alien, as in "ET" or "Close Encounters of the Third Kind". A third possibility, namely that highly advanced aliens, rather than trying to conquer us or to extend the hand of friendship to us, might want to study us in the way a human zoologist studies some particularly interesting species of animal, has not proved so popular as a plot line. (A fourth possibility, that the aliens might simply ignore us altogether, has remained altogether unexplored, probably because it would not make for a very exciting film). Klaatu, the alien at the centre of "The Day the Earth Stood Still" is (at least at first sight) a Friendly Alien. To be specific he is a member of a particular subspecies of the genre, the Friendly Alien who comes to save us from ourselves. Klaatu lands in the centre of Washington, D.C in his flying saucer, from which he emerges with his robot companion Gort. His arrival naturally causes a good deal of consternation among the citizenry, and also among the military, who seem to think that Klaatu is the advance guard of an invasion by those dastardly Commies. In political circles, however, there is less interest. Although Klaatu announces that he has come to deliver an important message to all of humanity, the President does not put in an appearance. (A cool customer, that Harry S Truman). Instead Klaatu is greeted by a White House official who treats him with the sort of condescending politeness with which he might address a minor diplomat from some third-rate power. One might suppose that the arrival of alien emissaries were an everyday event in Washington and that most of Klaatu's predecessors in the role had proved something of a disappointment. Unable to gain any assistance from men of affairs, Klaatu turns his attention to men of science, and seeks out the famous scientist Professor Jacob Barnhardt (a thinly disguised Albert Einstein) who proves more amenable to his message. He also decides to find out more about the race among whom he is living by lodging incognito at a boarding house under the name of Carpenter, befriending an attractive young war widow, Helen Benson, and her son Bobby, whom he impresses by solving a problem in physics which has baffled even Barnhardt. As Klaatu looks indistinguishable from a human being and speaks impeccable English with an upper-class British accent, this deception is not difficult for him to pull off. Scriptwriter Edmund H. North seems to have had a lot of fun inserting subtle, and sometimes not-so-subtle, comparisons between Klaatu and Jesus Christ. The name "Carpenter" (Christ is traditionally believed to have worked as a carpenter) is perhaps one of the more subtle ones, but there are some more obvious parallels; Klaatu has seemingly miraculous powers- the title derives from an incident when he demonstrates them by neutralising all electric power around the globe- and even rises from the dead. Indeed, he was too much of a Christ-figure for the liking of Joseph Breen, America's chief film censor in 1951, and North was compelled to insert a line about the "Almighty Spirit", showing that Klaatu's people recognise a Supreme Being and that he himself does not claim divinity.And then the time comes for Klaatu to deliver his message. We have been led to believe that it will be something to do with peace and brotherhood, and so it proves, but the message itself comes as something of a surprise, and it is not as friendly or as Christ-like as we might have expected. Although Klaatu has boasted of his people's superior morality and rationality, it transpires that peace is maintained among the federation to which his planet belongs not by the exercise of reason or virtue but by deterrence. The federation has created an all-powerful race of robots- Gort is one of these galactic Robocops- to act as a sort of police force and to keep the peace by punishing anyone guilty of acts of aggression, up to and including the destruction of their own planet. Klaatu and his people are not too worried if the Earthlings fight among themselves, but they are solemnly warned that if they should try and export their aggression into Outer Space their world will be reduced to a burned-out cinder, just see if I don't. So there we have it. Deterrence works. Those who have praised the film for its "timeless message of peace" or who have condemned it as pinko leftist pacifist propaganda seem to me to have missed the point. "The Day the Earth Stood Still" can be seen as a vindication of the theory of the nuclear balance of power or of the war then being waged by the United Nations against communist aggression in Korea. It could even, in fact, be interpreted as a justification of the atomic bombing of Japan or the conventional bombing of Germany in response to the aggression perpetrated by the leaders of those two countries. Whether the film- makers actually intended these interpretations is another matter, but it does go to show that symbolism and allegory can be two-edged swords, allowing for more than one meaning. Yet, quite possibly, North and director Robert Wise did indeed intend their film to have this ambiguous double meaning. It certainly makes for a more intriguing film than an unambiguous pacifist sermon would have done. 7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Hard Sci-fi and the Fluffy Robot</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Advertised as a frightening alien invasion movie about the end of days, the mantelpiece of 1950s science fiction on-screen fare "The Day the Earth Stood Still", manages to do what almost no other genre feature has managed to do. Survive the CGI revolution intact mainly thanks to its stunning and periodically relevant story, delivered by a memorable role of Michael Rennie as Klaatu / Mr. Carpenter, the extra-terrestial being, who visits Earth to deliver humankind a stern warning about their persistent path to self-annihilation. Met with fear and systemic governmental denial Klaatu becomes a runaway, attempting to deliver the message despite obstinacy of our stubborn ruling class.Set in the Cold War era of nuclear weapons and mutually assured destruction, the theme continues to resound thanks in part to the permeating sense of relevance in the paranoid time period, unlike the hollow remake with Keanu Reeves, which comes off as drab moralising. Here the threat is real - with the ideological divide drawn out as a petty squabble between bickering children, but which consequences are of a global scale. Showing the closed-mindedness of power, which seems to prefer penis-envy destruction and extrapolating foes over common sense and a willingness to create above differences, "The Day the Earth Stood Still" still rings true. A stark reminder that once the powers-to-be were basically ready to inflict global extermination of the human race as to protect the power caused by systemic differences. And uncovering the triviality of the Cold War power struggle remains the movies biggest input into world cinema.And not even the fluffy robot of apocalypse can change that...</fullReview>

		<date>2010-11-11</date>
		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>They Came To Warn Us!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not only did director Robert Wise's seminal, science fiction saga "The Day The Earth Stood Still" espouse traditional American values and depict Cold War tensions, but the atmospheric, black &amp; white film also influenced a generation of alien invasion movies. Most early sci-fi movies occurred primarily on planet Earth. A mysterious flying saucer lands in Washington, D.C., and an eloquent spokesman from another planet warns earthlings that they must curb their destructive impulses. Wise and "Only the Valiant" scenarist Edmund North forged the first truly exceptional sci-fi film of the 1950s with "The Day The Earth Stood Still." Solid production values distinguished this far-fetched but intriguing thriller. Basically, aliens land on Earth and a trigger-happy military wants to obliterate them. The idea that an alien would masquerade as a human to try and understand our mind-set and culture before it passes judgment on mankind is pretty liberal. Wise manages to depict a public spectacle from an objective perspective as well as relate a smaller, more intimate story about the relationship between the alien and a single parent mother and her impressionable son. Despite its suspenseful doomsday plot, "The Day The Earth Stood Still" isn't a humorless bore. Choice lines of dialogue and the reactions which they elicit give the film a sly sense of humor. One item which enhances the atmosphere here is "Citizen Kane" composer Bernard Herrman's use of an electronic musical instrument called the Theremin that evokes an uneasy feeling. The bell-shaped spaceship with its retracting passageway and the looming hulk of a robot became iconic symbols that other filmmakers would pay tribute to in their variations on the plot. Clearly, "The Day The Earth Stood Still" exemplifies those sci-fi films about benevolent aliens who are not committed to the wholesale destruction of mankind. Interestingly enough, Twentieth Century Fox could not persuade the Pentagon to contribute U.S. Army vehicles so the company persuaded the National Guard to take up the slack and provide them with tanks, artillery and troops. Wise learned about the project from studio chieftain Darryl F. Zanuck who recommended him to see "Broken Arrow" producer Julian Blaustein. According to Wise, Zanuck wasn't as interested in producing a socially relevant thriller as much as he was intent on making an audience pleaser. "The Day The Earth Stood Still" opens with several shots of Earth as a giant flying saucer moving at supersonic speed arouses the attention of radar monitors. The exotic looking spacecraft lands in Washington, D.C. on the mall near the Washington Monument and a tall slender man in a space suit, Klaatu (Michael Rennie of "The Black Rose"), emerges to find his craft surrounded by the U.S. Army. He announces to the people crowded around his saucer that he has come in peace. He leaves the saucer and approaches the crowd with an object in his fist that sprouts spikes of some kind. Every soldier has their rifles and pistols trained on Klaatu, and one soldier in a tank shoots Klaatu. He obliterates the object in Klaatu's fist and wounds him. An even taller robot appears and emits a beam from its helmet-like head and melts rifles, tanks and artillery pieces and then does nothing. The military rush Klaatu to Walter Reed Hospital. Klaatu recovers and tells a representative from the President that he wants to meet all the leaders of the world. The representative explains that such a meeting is entirely impossible. Klaatu wants to leave, but the military refuse to allow him to leave so he vanishes later on his own in a suit of clothes that belong to a Major Carpenter. Carpenter roams the streets of Washington incognito while news of his escape covers the airwaves. Klaatu enters a boarding house and obtains a room. He meets Mrs. Benson (Patricia Neal of "Breakfast at Tiffany's") and her young son Billy Gray (Bobby Benson of "Bad Men of Tombstone") as well as the others who live in the boarding house. Helen's boyfriend, an insurance agent Tom Stevens (Hugh Marlowe of "Garden of Evil"), arrives to take Helen out and Helen asks if Carpenter will watch out for Billy. Billy and Carpenter stroll around Washington and Carpenter shows Billy a fistful of diamonds that he uses for currency. They visit Professor Jacob Barnhardt (Sam Jaffe of "Gunga Din"), but he isn't home. Carpenter leaves Barnhardt a calling card and fixes an equation that the professor has been toiling over in the realm of celestial mechanics. Later, Barnhardt sends a man to fetch Carpenter and they discuss the problem that the universe is facing. Earthlings have obtain the weapons of atomic power and now they pose a threat to the rest of the universe and Carpenter has come to warn them that the rest of the universe is prepared to destroy them if they send out atomic spaceships. Bobby follows Carpenter when the man heads back to the flying saucer and uses a flashlight to communicate with the robot Gort. By now the military are tracking Carpenter and they shoot him. Before Carpenter died, he warned Helen to rush to Gort and repeat a simple three word phrase to prevent Gort from destroying the world. Gort breaks into a jail and takes Carpenter's body back to the spacecraft and revives it. Carpenter emerges and warns the Earth one more time about its aggressive tendencies and then departs."The Day The Earth Stood Still" is an intelligent sci-fi adventure with a capable cast. Wise maintains suspense throughout the film's 92 minute running time. This was Michael Rennie's big break; he had been making films in England before Zanuck discovered him. Although it would probably bore audiences today, "The Day The Earth Stood Still" remains a cinematic landmark in terms of science fiction.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>MBunge</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Be very careful what you wish for</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I really thought someone in Hollywood needed to do a remake of this movie, and that's not something I suggest lightly. Mainstream American movies are drowning in a shallow pool of unoriginality. The box office has been dominated for years by remakes and big screen adaptations of comic books and old TV shows. We see sequels to mediocre movies and sequels like Superman Returns and the new Indiana Jones that are coming out 20 years after the previous films. They spent a couple hundred million dollars doing a big screen version of Speed Racer, a 40 year old Japanese cartoon that most Americans have never even seen. That's how desperate and fixated Hollywood is on doing movies based on things that have worked before. But, The Day the Earth Stood Still is one of those rare works that deserves to be refashioned and reimagined for a new era and a new audience. It's because the story is still valid, it still speaks to essential elements of the human condition in a way that's not likely to be improved upon. It's especially because science fiction movies seem to have forgotten the initial point of the whole genre. The Day the Earth Stood Still tells the story of Klaatu, an alien who is bringing a great and terrible message to humanity. Coming in a single space ship and accompanied only by his 8 foot tall robot pal Gort, Klaatu lands in Washington D.C?.and promptly gets shot in the shoulder by a panicky U.S. soldier. While recovering in the hospital, Klaatu learns that the rest of the world can't put aside their fears and ambitions and resentments long enough to listen to his world-shaking message. So he escapes the hospital to get a closer look at humanity, settling in as a guest in an old fashioned boarding house. One of the best things about the film is that it demonstrates both the strengths and weaknesses of mankind, without amplifying them into exaggerated cartoons. Characters aren't portrayed as unrealistically good or irrationally fearful and selfish, yet the audience can clearly understand what Klaatu is both afraid and hopeful of in our planet. The Day the Earth Stood Still is largely remembered for its political and social daring. Making a movie in the early 1950s where the hero is an extraterrestrial standing in judgment of all Earth was pretty bold. Telling audiences that the solution to war and conflict isn't conquest or victory but submission of sovereignty, in the aftermath of World War II and the birth pangs of the Cold War, wasn't exactly following the tenor of the times. It's a provocative and thoughtful message that shouldn't be allowed to recede into history. Which is why The Day the Earth Stood Still should have been remade for modern audiences and freed from the burden of history. This movie reflects the rhythms of an American that was largely pre-television, let alone pre-internet. It was an American that was less cynical or less self-ware, depending on your perspective. I worry that kids growing up in today's America, where it seems they're not left alone with their own thoughts for 5 seconds at a time, simply won't tolerate the more measured and reflective tone of this film. That's the sort of remakes that Hollywood should be doing, ones that take great stories and make them palatable to new generations. Klaatu Barada Nikto aren't words that only 50 year old sci-fi geeks should understand.Of course, then they went and did a remake and I realize they should have left well enough alone.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Calling Occupants of Interplanetary Craft</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film very effectively answers the question, "Why don't 'flying saucers' ever land in Washington, DC and ask to speak to the President?" Silver-suited Michael Rennie (as Klaatu) lands in plain sight, and emerges from his spaceship in full view of everyone, along with his powerful robot companion "Gort" (Lock Martin). Although Mr. Rennie states, "We have come to visit you in peace," he oddly invites a trigger-happy American to shoot from the hip.Rennie is taken to the hospital, wounded. Then, he makes his demand. Rennie wants all of Earth's representatives to meet him for an important message. When that proves too daunting a task, Rennie escapes from watchful hospital eyes. To soak up local color, he takes the identity "L.M. Carpenter" and goes to live in a local boarding house. There, Rennie meets some typical Americans, including World War II widow Patricia Neal (as Helen Benson) and her Billy Gray (as Bobby). With young Gray's help, Rennie tries to arrange a meeting with brilliant Professor Sam Jaffe (as Jacob Barnhardt).Meanwhile, Ms. Neal's fiancé, Hugh Marlowe (Tom Stevens), becomes suspicious of Rennie. Although Rennie often seems powerful enough to deliver his message without all the problems he encounters, the suspense makes it a much more exciting film. It's also very cleverly written, by Edmund H. North, with an interesting mix of the obvious and the subtle; for example, "Carpenter" refers to "the almighty spirit" - but, when you expect Gray to kneel and pray, he pulls out a train set. Director Robert Wise, photographer Leo Tover, and especially soundtrack musician Bernard Herrmann really make it hum.********* The Day the Earth Stood Still (9/18/51) Robert Wise ~ Michael Rennie, Patricia Neal, Billy Gray, Sam Jaffe</fullReview>

		<username>sddavis63 (revsdd@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Pretty Decent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I haven't seen the 2008 remake of this title, partly because I wanted to make sure I had the original in my mind when I did, and it had been a long time since I had seen this. Considering the limitations it faced in 1951, it's a pretty decent sci-fi story. In the modern world, of course, sci-fi has become virtually synonymous with action-adventure and ridiculously costumed aliens and shoot 'em out gunfights (or laser fights, or phaser fights, or whatever.) There's really little of that here. This movie deals with story and characters rather than action, there are few ridiculous costumes (although Gort's make-up is really quite unconvincing as a robot, the scenes where his visor opens are quite effective in making him a threatening character) and aside from Klaatu being shot by us (ie, humans) the only thing remotely resembling gun fighting is some sort of laser beam Gort uses to disintegrate weapons. The story, to be honest, is pretty basic and to the point. Klaatu arrives to tell us that humanity is warlike and violent (now there's a shock) and that unless we stop it we'll be destroyed for the sake of the peace of the universe. The arrival of the aliens is problematic. Although it introduces excitement right off the top, we're told that Klaatu spent five months travelling 250 million miles to earth. If my math is right, then he could only have come from Mercury, Venus or Mars. Setting aside the fact that we now know that there's no intelligent life on any of those planets, taking five months to go 250 million miles is pretty slow, space travel-wise. With travel speeds like that, one wonders how there could be any sort of inter-planetary association the likes of which Klaatu speaks. In any event, the movie revolves around Klaatu's interactions with various humans. After being shot, he escapes from the hospital and adopts the identity of "Mr. Carpenter" - to learn more about human beings. My impression was that, while he developed some bonds (primarily with Bobby and his mother and with Professor Barnhart), he wasn't overly impressed with us, and the movie ends with an open-ended restatement of the message: "stop it, or else!"It's not quite the classic that it's made out by some to be, but it is a decent story, and a good reminder that science fiction doesn't have to have all the bells and whistles that we generally see now. 7/10</fullReview>

Made during the Cold War, this drama about a space alien named Klaatu (Michael Rennie) trying to warn the entire planet Earth about the dangers of continued aggression against the enemy can still pack a wallop in terms of how we the people still haven't found a common ground of achieving lasting peace all around. Other players like Hugh Marlowe as Mrs. Benson's initial boyfriend Tom Stevens and especially Sam Jaffe as Professor Jacob Barnhardt also make good impressions, performance-wise, in addition to the above cast I cited. And that Bernard Herrmann score being done with both higher and lower pitch theremins must have sounded really cool when first presented on cinema screens back in the early '50s! So on that note, The Day the Earth Stood Still comes highly recommended. Oh, and "Klaatu barada nikto"!</fullReview>

Amazing how the actuality of this film has not aged a single day since its release in 1951. In ways, this was a visionary film.
