		<shortDescription>Sci-fi and the Cold War</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Day the Earth Stood Still is considered on of the best sci-fi movies to come from the 1950s, standing along side Invasion of the Body Snatchers. It not just serve as a film for sci-fi fans, but also a commentary of the Cold War.A lone alien Klaatu (Michael Rennie) land his flying saucer in the middle of Washington D.C., with a message of good-will and peace, but get shot by a nervous solider. When he asks to meet all of Earth's leaders his request is met with death, so escapes from the American military's custody. On the run Klaatu hides in a boarding house and befriends Helen Benson (Patricia Neal) and her young son Bobby (Billy Gray). Seeing the short-term nature of humanity who see the alien as a threat he decides he needs to give the Earth a warning to ensure their listen to him. He has to do it before his robot protector Gort (Lock Martin) attacks humanity.The Day the Earth Stood Still has had an enduring legacy, having been remade in 2008 and been constantly referred to in Alan Moore's Watchmen. It was not only a film that wanted to be a big spectacle but a more thoughtful film about the nature of mankind and it's short-sighted nature and a commentary about the Cold War and the nuclear arms race, which would have led to the end of live as we know it.The special effects and action sequences are cheesy, particularly by today's standards but the director Richard Wise compensates through his characters and overall themes. His direction is more subtle looking at the world through Klaatu's eyes, with simple looks at the characters and their reactions. This is a film with likable characters where no one is treated as truly evil. The scene when Klaatu makes his final speech is particularly memorable.The acting of the main cast is decent, pretty standard for the period. Gray was not a particularly good child actor. But Rennie was very strong in the lead role as an alien not use to Earth's customs, a creature who is logical but believes in his cause. He is far from being a dull character.Obviously this is a film that does stretch believable logic, but it is worth watching.</fullReview>

For the drama genre, the influences of films today root from "Citizen Kane" and "12 Angry Men". The family friendly films root from the first Disney-made movies. For the sci fi genre today, everything roots from "The Day the Earth Stood Still"."Star Wars", "Star Trek", and most definitely "Plan 9 from Outer Space" have gotten a piece of "TDTESS" in their plots. So well educated this race is that war is not an option.Second, he comes with a warning. This warning is in the form of preventing space exploration because a bunch of indestructible robots will destroy earth.Hollywood is in the production of remaking this classic for a new generation. Personally, it seems hard to replace "space exploration" with "destruction of ozone layer" or "communism" with "terrorism". I think remaking this will only accomplish 2 things; taking away its original morals for Michael Bay-ish boom booms and making the quick dollar off of people hoping for the same movie.I don't give movies 10 stars unless it inspires others. This did.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Patrick Johns Humanities 10 8-29-04 Movie ReviewThe movie we watched in class, The Day the Earth Stood Still, was interesting in two main ways. This movie contradicted itself many times but the point of the movie still applies to today's world. Klaatu believed in no violence but he would fight violence with violence. The point of the movie, which was to live in peace or there will be consequences, has still not been accomplished in today's world.This movie contradicted itself many times about violence. Klaatu's speech states, 'We have and organization for the mutual protection of all planets and for the complete elimination of aggression.' He contradicts himself further by saying, 'It is no concern of ours how you run your own planet, but if you threaten to extend your violence, this Earth of yours will be reduced to a burned-out cinder.' Klaatu during the movie brags many times about how his planet is free from violence and they all live in peace. However, Klaatu now says he will fight violence with violence. This means his planet is not totally free from violence and peace. Also, the policemen from his planet act only when they see violence. This is another example that people on his planet are still being violent or they would not have a need for policemen in the first place.The theme of this movie is humans need to find a way to live in complete peace and without violence. If we don't live in peace we may end up destroying ourselves. This goal is still trying to be accomplished in today's world. This goal may take millions and millions of years to complete but we need to make progress every single day. It's like Klaatu said, if we don't start living in peace and without violence, there will be consequences. Character list: 1. Klaatu (man from outer space also known as Mr. Carpenter). Played by Michael Rennie. 2. Helen Benson played by Patricia Neal. 3. Tom Stevens played by Hugh Marlowe. Insurance salesmen and Helen's boyfriend. 4. Bobby Benson played by Billy Gray. 5. Professor Barnhardt played by Sam Jaffe. A very brilliant scientist. 6. Mrs. Barley played by Frances Bavier. A guest in the boarding house. 7. Gort played by Lock Martin. Klaatu's robot. 8. Mr. Harley played by Frank Conroy. The Secretary of Defense. 9. Gabriel Heater, Elmer Davis, Drew Pearson, and H.V. Kaltenborn play themselves.</fullReview>

		<username>SonOfMoog</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The SETI Phone Rings, and ..</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is told in three parts. The first, and weakest of the three, is an affected, clumsy study in mob behavior. The spaceship lands, Klaatu appears, and every reaction to his appearance is either an attempt to get closer out of some lurid curiosity, or suspicious fear bordering on paranoia. The alien Klaatu is shot within moments of disembarking his ship.There is some real truth here about why we might not want to answer the SETI telephone when and if it rings, but it is shallow, unsophisticated and undeveloped. The middle is a standard caper flick with the alien and the boy who befriends him on the run, as it were, seeing the sights of Washington DC. They walk everywhere. While it's possible to do that, I know because I have, it's not particularly plausible. When my attention is drawn to insignificant little details like that, the details are not insignificant.The final third, everything from the blackout to closing credits, is the sci-fi thriller we all love. Bernard Herrmann's score is eerie, atonal, and annoying early-on, trying to build mystery, mood and suspense, I suppose. It undermined rather than developed the suspense, but in the last part of the film, the music changed, and was much more effective. Part Three has many of the parts we love: the worldwide blackout; Gort on the prowl, vaporizing two guards (a moment when we suddenly realize Klaatu's quest is deadly serious business); Klaatu's death and resurrection, and that wonderful line about the "Almighty Spirit" associated with it; and, of course, the magnificent closing soliloquy: "Join us .. or be obliterated.!"Michael Rennie is terrific here. Yes, he is wooden, unable to move, it would appear, from the waist up, but that stiffness is a very believable quality in this role as an alien trying to pass as human. I thought Sam Jaffe as the Professor, and Frank Conroy (uncredited) as the President's Secretary were also very good in supporting roles. 7 out of 10, but I wanted to like it so much more.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2010-12-06</date>
		<username>Coventry</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Listen to the Wise Alien!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is considered as one of the greatest landmarks in Sci-Fi cinema, and righteously so, as it epitomizes plentiful of trademarks directly linked to the genre. The film baths in an atmosphere of mass paranoia and fear, but still the script is more of a political statement rather than a story about planetary invasion. "The Day the Earth Stood Still" was one of the very first Sci-Fi classics to come out in the 1950's ? a decade famous amongst genre fanatics for all its versatile outings ? and still remains one of the most solid ones, thanks to the heavyweight morals in the script and the surefooted direction of Robert Wise. Still relatively shortly after the end of WWII, this movie came with an ambitious and definitely admirable message. Superior alien races, with technologies and medical wisdom that is far more advanced than ours, put the entire earthly population to an ultimatum: stop all the senseless political turmoil and particularly the development of nuclear warfare, if not we will all face the disastrous consequences. The transfer of this message already goes horribly wrong, as the humanoid alien Klaatu is immediately gunned down by nervous military men as soon as he lands his flying saucer in the middle of Washington D.C. Humanity is even more petrified when Klaatu's ten ft. tall and indestructible pet robot Gort emerges from the spaceship and vaporizes the earthly artillery. Klaatu subsequently dismantles his robot and decides to go 'undercover' for a while, renting a room as Mr. Carpenter and living with a widow and her inquisitive 12-year-old son. "The Day The Earth Stood Still" might have lost a little bit of its impact over the past sixty years (not a whole lot though), it contains numerous sequences that are timelessly classic and forever will be, like the global power breakdown and ? of course ? the legendary "Klaatu Barada Nikto" words. It's simply a great film with a wonderful cast and many of the best dialogs ever written. If you look at the film with skeptical post-2000 eyes, you'll find plentiful of charming defaults (like extremely slow-moving robots moving around half of Washington without being spotted) and primitive special effects, but it's all part of its bona fide classic status.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2009-03-16</date>
		<username>Steffi_P</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>"That power cannot be revoked"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Although science fiction cinema can be said to go back as far as Melies and his Trip to the Moon, it took a long time to establish its character and clichés. The Day the Earth Stood Still has earned its place in history for turning sci-fi into a genre in its own right.One thing that makes The Day the Earth Stood Still different from earlier sci-fi pictures is that it adds an element of horror. It not only attempts to terrify us with the thought an extra-terrestrial threat, it actually borrows techniques from the horror genre to unsettle us. Director Robert Wise's earliest assignments were at Val Lewton's B-unit at RKO, and he had slipped in touches of Lewton-esquire horror atmospherics, where appropriate, into the handful of thrillers and Westerns he had made since then. He subtly continues this pattern here, adding layers of creepiness to many scenes with low-key lighting, distant vanishing points and looming framing devices. Bernard Hermann's theremin score compliments this mood.The Day the Earth Stood Still is also unusual in that its story is told on a global scale, and needs to show events going on simultaneously all over the world ? something that was quite unusual in cinema prior to this. This is where Robert Wise's background as an editor comes in. Although no longer cutting the pictures himself, he shows awareness of what an editor needs. Take those opening scenes in which the spaceship is first spotted ? the sense of movement and lighting in each shot seems aimed at making each one compliment the next. The actual editor William Reynolds (one of the best in the business; he also did the job on The Godfather) uses Wise's material to put together some powerful sequences, expertly building up the intensity as panic spreads across the globe, or dropping back down to an eerie calm.You can see why people love The Day the Earth Stood Still. There is power and profundity to the message, the plot is nicely balanced and the analogy to the Jesus story is well-defined but not overstated (although funnily Klaatu's ultimatum is more reminiscent of the Old Testament God who sent the deluge). And yet on closer inspection it does have its flaws. The dialogue is fairly trite and some moments are just daft. The acting is mediocre, and it seems faintly ridiculous how obvious Michael Rennie's RADA accent is when he speaks in the alien language. Like Fight Club or Memento today, this is one of those "clever" films that wows people first time round, but won't seem so amazing on repeated viewings. It may be an important picture in cinema history, and it is certainly memorable, but it isn't a brilliantly put together package and doesn't hold up as entertainment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Neil Doyle</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Soft spot in my heart for this fun-filled sci-fi film from the past...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>THE DAY THE EARTH STOOD STILL may have lost some of its punch in this day of CGI generated thrills, and it always was a rather slow moving story that gradually builds to an interesting climax--but it has a soft spot in my heart as one of the first sci-fi thrillers that got me interested in this genre.PATRICIA NEAL claims that she felt awfully silly uttering some of her lines spoken to Klaatu and I don't blame her. In fact, when it comes right down to it (and seen from a different perspective years later), the reaction to this space alien all seems rather overdone and on the plus side of being unbelievable. But it still manages to hold the interest because everyone in the cast seems to believe in the story with solemn faces and earnest performances.MICHAEL RENNIE is quietly effective and dignified as Klaatu, and HUGH MARLOWE, SAM JAFFE and little BILLY GRAY are satisfying in supporting roles. Robert Wise directs with a flair for this sort of thing and it's just plain fun to watch the tale unfold in almost documentary fashion. It's no WAR OF THE WORLDS, but it's sci-fi fun that still clicks.</fullReview>

If they do not succeed in this, then they will be destroyed in result from possibly endangering other planets. My overall personal opinion of the movie was positive. I enjoyed this film, even though I don't really care at all for Sci-Fi movies or old ones in general. I was pleasantly surprised to find that even though this movie was made in 1951, it still can make sense in our world today.Unlike other Sci-Fi or thriller movies in the market today, this movie made the aliens out to be the 'good guys'. They came in peace, and their purposeful destination was to bring a helpful suggestion to stop violence all together on Earth. Klaatu, one of the main characters, was made to seem just like a real human being. The actor, Michael Rennie, did a fine job on the acting. For this movie being made in 1951, the special affects were surprisingly good and not too weird. Another great aspect of this film was the music. The suspense-building music gave the movie a bonus. The little extras of this movie pulled 'The Day the Earth Stood Still' together.Like all movies, there were also negative parts to it. I personally did not care for the robot's costume. This action is a little too harsh and strong for me.Overall I would rate this film as 7 out of 10. For a movie that I would not normally pick out for myself, I liked it a lot better then I would have expected to. I liked how the movie, being so old, actually goes along with the world today. Besides some of the parts during the ending, this movie kept my attention and was thoroughly enjoyed.</fullReview>

This here is probably the film that kick started the countless sci-fi flicks that would follow throughout the decade. While there have been countless imitators there's no denying that this is the greatest of them all and the simple nature of the story also makes it one of the best films ever made. The simple nature of the story is the reason I think the film works so well because instead of some silly, over the top storyline we've got something that comes off as real and not fiction. The simple nature of the storytelling also makes the film seem all the more realistic and, again, this here makes the film come off more as drama than some silly fiction. The thought of the world's fighting could cause damage to other planets and then having those planets attack is a pretty easy thing to think about and understand. Rennie never gets enough credit but his performance also brings realism to the film that helps it. He has to give several speeches about how bad things could get on Earth and a lesser performance would have had these scenes come off as campy but that's not the case here. Patricia Neal also comes off very good in her supporting role. Another amazing thing about this film is how it's story is pretty much timeless and could work in any decade. The film also benefits from Bernard Herrmann's brilliant music score, which in my opinion is one of the greatest ever made.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>So that was "The Andy Griffith Show"'s Aunt Bee playing Mrs. Barley?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Robert Wise's recent death brings "The Day the Earth Stood Still" to mind. Some of the things in the movie are clearly a product of the '50s, namely the public's blind trust of the government (made evident early in the movie when the people crowd around the spacecraft without any protection; for all that anyone knew, that spacecraft could have been radioactive). But still, this story of alien Klaatu (Michael Rennie) coming to Earth to warn humanity about the dangers of war seems especially relevant given the current state of the world. Patricia Neal is particularly good as Helen Benson, an every-woman who takes Klaatu into her apartment (without knowing who he is). And Klaatu's wordless cohort Gort is really something, as is the now famous line "Klaatu barada nikto." All in all, "TDTESS" isn't your average sci-fi movie. There are no scenes of scared crowds running away from something, or rays blowing up buildings. In fact, it might not suffice to call this a sci-fi movie. I don't want to use the phrase "social commentary", but it's something like that. We could all learn something from Klaatu.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Klaatu barada...Aw, why bother</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Oh my, is this movie silly. Well intentioned, but dumb. That's why, of the 4 points I gave to it, 2 go just for good intentions. But no wonder the yanks are P.O. As the story drags on we see that still Klaatu doesn't have a clue about our world as he insists--with his usual condescending tone--that he must see...well, his expectations are a bit lower by now as he would accept instead philosophers and scientists. Funny thing, I got the same message from Clockwork Orange and they didn't need a spaceship to deliver it. So, at the end Klaatu has bothered to travel half a billion miles both ways just to show us he's not much different from us; that if we come to displease him, or rather his stormtroopers, they'll zap us to oblivion. If that's the Galactic Order, I can do without. Klaatu barada...Aw, why bother</fullReview>

		<date>2015-07-15</date>
		<username>disinterested_spectator</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>A Necker-Cube Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Whenever aliens from outer space visit our planet, there are three possibilities. The first is that the aliens are good, but we want to kill them because we just don't understand them, as in "It Came from Outer Space" (1953). The second is that the aliens are evil and we have to defend ourselves, as in "Earth vs. the Flying Saucers" (1956). All alien invasion plots were one or the other for a long time. Eventually, a third possibility emerged in which the aliens are good and we get along with them just fine, as in the movie "Close Encounters of the Third Kind" (1977)."The Day the Earth Stood Still" is a strange combination of the first two. Klaatu is an alien from an unspecified planet in our solar system. He has come to destroy every creature on this planet, if we don't do what he says. That's every creature, mind you, not just man, but animals too. So, he is evil and we need to destroy him, right? Wrong! Like a Necker cube, at various times the movie flips and it is Klaatu who is good while we are in the wrong. Helen Benson says that the spaceman may not be a menace at all, and that he is just afraid. The movie approves of her sentiment. Her fiancé, Tom Stevens, believes that the spaceman is a menace, but the movie wants us to regard him as narrow-minded and prejudiced. As a result, we get this incongruous depiction of an alien who is kind and lovable and good, but who will wipe out our entire planet if we don't obey him.What he insists upon, it turns out, is that we not extend out violent ways into space, thereby threatening his civilization and that of other planets. Because the movie is set contemporaneously, the year is 1951, and it will be several years before the Soviets even manage to launch Sputnik. So we are not about to extend our violence any time soon. But never mind that. Klaatu says he is losing patience and may have to level New York to get everyone's attention. That sounds pretty evil, but then the Necker cube flips, and he is seen having a warm conversation with Professor Barnhardt, who does not seem to be the least appalled by Klaatu's threats to kill everybody.Barnhardt, you see, is a scientist. In a right-wing movie, it is the government along with the military and the police who are good, while the scientists are dangerous, because they want to communicate with the aliens or study them. But this is a left-wing movie, where scientists are good and politicians are bad, or at least pigheaded and narrow-minded. Barnhardt agrees to get scientists from all over the world to listen to Klaatu (apparently it takes a scientist to understand Klaatu's threat to destroy all life on this planet).Earlier in the movie, when the Necker cube flips and Klaatu is basically a good alien, he decides to find out what earthlings are like. He seems impressed by the goodness of humans; he is deeply moved by the words of Lincoln; and he is fond of Helen and Bobby. But then the Necker cube flips back, and he is once again threatening us with destruction, as if we were the scourge of the universe, and that includes Helen and Bobby.Barnhardt suggests that instead of leveling New York, Klaatu should demonstrate his power without hurting anyone. Klaatu does this by neutralizing electricity all over the world, causing the electricity to go off, not only in buildings but also in engines, excepting only airplanes and hospitals. During the blackout, Klaatu and Helen are trapped on an elevator. By the time the power comes back on, he has confided in her and now she trusts him. "He's not a menace," she says to Tom. But Tom thinks Klaatu is a menace, which he clearly is. So, to keep us from siding with Tom, he is revealed to be an unpleasant character. He lets Helen know that telling the army where they can find Klaatu will allow him to write his own ticket, be the biggest man in the country, get his picture in all the papers, and be a hero. We should have suspected something like this when we found out he was a life insurance salesman. Needless to say, that engagement is off.Finally, Klaatu delivers his message, saying that Gort is one of a race of robots who patrol the planets. If any planet steps out of line, it is incinerated. And they are like a doomsday machine, which cannot be turned off. Then Klaatu and Gort fly away.As I said, this is a left-wing movie. Conservatives have always been suspicious of the United Nations, fearing a loss of sovereignty, accusing liberals of wanting our nation to submit to some supranational government in charge of a new world order. This takes that idea to the next level. Now there is a kind of United Planets, and it is Earth that has lost its sovereignty. The attitude of this movie is that this is a good thing.I have always fantasized about a sequel, in which Americans capture one of the flying saucers with a robot on board, and reverse engineer them until we have an army of robots and a fleet of flying saucers of our own, which we then use to destroy all those arrogant aliens and colonize their planets. At the end of the movie, with America triumphant, the president gives a victory speech, saying that our new manifest destiny has been achieved because we are a God-fearing people.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Jonathan C</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Surprise, Its Awesome</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I watched The Day the Earth Stood Still for the first time this past week. I can honestly say that I was shocked--this movie is really good, and not just as tacky entertainment. It has a clever idea, a message, and a follow-through that are all well-conceived. The special effects are, well, primitive, but this is 1951 after all.For me, there were three phases to watching the movie. In the first phase, we get a dose of the tacky special effects as a flying saucer lands on the mall in Washington DC and a robot from the old Lost in Space TV series comes out. At this point, we think, uh oh, this is a prequel to Plan Nine from Outer Space--should be good for a few laughs.We then get introduced to the main character, Klaatu, who is an alien with a message of peace. He is definitely a friendly alien, and wants to achieve his aims without violence. The goal is to warn the earth--live in peace or face obliteration, and as he tries to spread his message, he is faced with fearful people who want to capture and kill him.It is at this point where we enter the second phase of viewing the movie, when we realize he is an allegory for Jesus (Mr. Carptenter!). We then go UH OH, they aren't really going there, are they? We settle back for the train wreck that we think will happen.And then the miracle occurs--the train wreck does not happen. There are amazing scenes--like when the alien views with sadness and compassion the war dead at Arlington Cemetery, including the father of the small boy he is babysitting for the day. The frightful and cool scene where the alien peacefully demonstrates his power over the Earth. The truly edgy car chase as the alien is pursued in the streets of Washington. The story gets going, the characters really come to life, and allegory works--it is powerful stuff. This movie is no joke--it is a classic with a message that I think even the subject of the allegory would have appreciated.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>So-So Sci-Fi</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>An alien spaceship lands in Washington and a man from another planet emerges with a message for Earthlings. This is a quintessential sci-fi from the 1950s, perhaps the peak decade for the genre. Rennie is perfectly cast as the alien, with his gaunt looks and tall, slender build. The plot is simplistic and silly of course, with little attempt at any logic, and the message is somewhat forced, a product of its times. It's solidly directed by Wise, but he overdoes the global reactions to the alien's actions. Herrmann provides an appropriately other-worldly score, although his best work was yet to come in Hitchcock's films.</fullReview>

		<username>orangewarm</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Destroying the earth - Again!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If media reflects its society's culture, then the Hollywood blockbuster, the most expensive and complex media around, may hold the key to Western society's deepest fears.Emerson said that "fiction reveals the truth that reality obscures." Some commentators (including Baudrillard) have said that Independence Day (1996) foresaw the events of 9/11 ? that it gave an insight into the subconscious hatred of American hegemony.I've just seen The Day The Earth Stood Still (2008), Keanu Reeve's latest sci-fi vehicle. It seems to me, that ever since Independence Day in 1996, Hollywood has been producing and exporting a wide variety of disaster movies which centre around the theme of "destruction of the earth." Communications Theory can be seen as an offshoot of Sociology ? what this means is that, for a Communications theorist, newspapers, magazines, books, television, cinema and advertising are all open to interpretation. The hope is that, by interpreting or deconstructing these media, we gain a deeper insight into our society, and possibly human nature.In the 50s, Hollywood was obsessed with aliens and monsters wreaking destruction upon the earth. These images are said to have been manufactured as a conscious effort in the propaganda war against Communism ? Communism being the destructive alien or monster.But it's too simplistic to say that in the 50s, Hollywood alien movies represented communism and now alien movies represent the threat of Islamic terrorism.The Day The Earth Stood Still is a remake of a 1951 movie, but the alien depicted in the original was hardly threatening. Actually, he brought with him a message of pacifism, calm and logic. His message was one of peace not subversion and war. Also, Independence Day was made before 9/11.The events of 9/11 temporarily abated film projects involving urban destruction, but only briefly. Spielberg's War of the Worlds (2005) and Cloverfield (2008) have definitive, obvious references to 9/11.There are several references to 9/11 in the The Day The Earth Stood Still: people running from smoke and dust in Manhattan, the dead father of the child in the film, who went to war to build, the child constantly shouting "why should we help them?" (the "them" being the aliens ? representing Muslims). More telling still, The Secretary of Defence now represents America ? not the President.The film reads like a party political broadcast for Greenpeace or Friends of the Earth, and is one a growing number of films including The Day After Tomorrow (2004), and M. Night Shyamalan's The Happening (2008), that purports to warn us about the dangers if we don't treat our planet right. Nature is literally fighting back in these films.What else do all these films have in common? They consistently show the destruction of structures. Starting with Independence Day, notice the buildings that get blown to smithereens: most notably The White House and The Empire State Building. The first building signifies the destruction of democracy, the second, the imminent demise of the American empire.In The Happening too, Shyamalan shows us that only the North East of America is affected ? why? The North East represents the centre of Finance, Print Media, Television and News in America. Along with California, the North East is the wealthiest part of the US. It is as if Shyamalan is saying, all these structures have gone to ****.The Brooklyn Bridge has been destroyed twice: once in I Am Legend (2007), and once in Cloverfield. Could this signify souring relations between those in Manhattan and those in Brooklyn? Or, is it as some critics have said, that "Manhattan is America" and the destruction of the bridge signifies a wider chasm of American separateness."Liberty" has been under attack several times: in The Day After Tomorrow, we see The Statue of Liberty buried in snow ? frozen; in Cloverfield, J. J. Abrams violently decapitates Liberty, hurling it like a weapon. Like in 1968, when we first saw Liberty damaged, buried in the sand in Planet of the Apes.Let us now look at the social representations in the films. Many of the films allude to the separation from loved ones: The Day after Tomorrow, The War of the Worlds, The Happening, Cloverfield, Independence Day, Armageddon (1998), I Am Legend and Deep Impact (1998) to name a few. But these films do not only show separation; they also reveal complex relationships and themes of adoption and surrogate parenting.In I Am Legend, Will Smith finds "a wife" and child that are not his own; in War of the Worlds, Tom Cruise is estranged from his children ? a useless dad. In Armageddon, Liv Tyler has no mother; in the Happening, Mark Walberg is "chasing" his own wife and is forced to look after a friend's child. In Independence Day, Will Smith is going out with an "exotic dancer" who has a child ? he has taken on the child as his own. In The Day The Earth Stood Still, it gets more complex still: the female lead is forced to look after a child that is not her own after the father and mother have died. What does this all mean?Hollywood would never have made films like these twenty years ago. What we can see is an obvious breaking down of the traditional family units of the last 100 years; traditional society is literally being torn apart, with a new restructuring of family and community taking shape.Former Mayor Ed Koch, who now keeps himself busy by reviewing movies for several newspapers in Manhattan, has attributed the persistence of the destruction theme to "edifice envy." "They want to see our skyscrapers destroyed because they are envious of them," Mr. Koch has said. We can only assume the "they" is the rest of the world.Then again, all these theories could just be a lot of bull.</fullReview>

		<username>ShootingShark</username>

		<shortDescription>You're A Long Way From Home, Aren't You Mr Carpenter ?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A flying saucer lands in Washington DC and an alien being named Klaatu emerges. His mission is to meet with heads of all Earth nations and deliver an ultimatum not to develop weapons capable of threatening outer space. Will mankind heed the warning ?Based on an great short story by Harry Bates and with a first-rate script, this terrific sci-fi classic (along with The Thing) kick-started the boom of exciting and imaginative fantasy films of the fifties. It's a three-act movie, each of which is engrossing and visually brilliant. Firstly, the UFO landing in the park and the appearance of Klaatu and his giant, foreboding, faceless robot Gort. Secondly, Billy and Mr Carpenter's thought-provoking odyssey through the city, which is my favourite part. And finally, the suspenseful conclusion as the military net closes in on Klaatu and he delivers his final message. It's a great yarn on several levels - as an escapist adventure, a global military-political treatise and a messianic allegory, complete with resurrection scene. Rennie is pretty unforgettable in the lead, playing the alien with stiff, polite sincerity, but also warmth, humour and trenchant purposefulness. Highly influential (see Starman particularly), with excellent photography throughout and a great creepy score by Bernard Herrmann. 6/10</fullReview>

		<date>2005-07-09</date>
		<username>Robert J. Maxwell (rmax304823@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Starry Messenger.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A superior example of the SF films so popular during the 1950s. Oh, the plot is full of holes and some of the characters are cardboard but it's enjoyable nonetheless and, more than that, thought provoking.Is the plot full of holes? Well, I don't really know exactly what that means, but the fact is that I have no idea how Klaatu (Michael Rennie) gets out of his locked room at Walter Reed Hospital without breaking through the door or the walls. I also don't know how it's possible for an 8-foot giant robot with a head the size of a beanbag chair to walk across the city of Washington, DC, melt the wall of a government building, pick up the dead body of an alien, and carry it back across town with no one bothering to notice. What do they do, pay Gort what sociologists call "civil inattention"? And how does Klaatu manage to open the door to Professor Barnhardt's study when Bobby can't? I should say that not all of the characters are cardboard. I was thinking mainly of Pat Neal's boyfriend Tom (Hugh Marlowe), a slimy creep from beginning to end. That mellifluous baritone didn't fool me for a minute. Just watch him light up with glee as he scopes that diamond! And his best scene -- shooing away Pat Neal while he squeals over the phone to the general about Klaatu's whereabouts, telling her, "Just wait till I get my picture in the papers. You'll feel differently." I had my picture in the papers once and look where I am now -- The Landru Home for the Criminally Insane, without possibility of grounds privileges! Rennie's Klaatu is not cardboard but is still a bit of a puzzle. Okay, his planet has been monitoring our transmissions for years. So Klaatu, as we would expect, is not only tall and handsome in an ectomorphic way but is gracious and polite as well. He knows all the interactional graces. ("Well, I'll say good-night again.") Well then how come he doesn't know the word "jittery" and isn't familiar with a music box? Huh? There's also a certain smugness about him that I'm not sure I cared for. He smiles and shakes his head with slight amusement at our stupidity.The kid wasn't much better, but I've always like Patricia Neal. Not sure why. She's not a knockout beauty and not a bravura actress but she seems to radiate a sort of integrity or stability that most of the rest of us could use a shot of from time to time.The message that Klaatu brings to Earth is an engaging one. No more of that aggression business on the geopolitical front. At least I think that's what his message is. He himself doesn't actually clarify it. If you people persist, we will destroy your rogue planet. On Klaatu's planet they have outlawed aggression and turned enforcement over to the interplanetary police, of which Gort is an example. The powers of the police cannot be revoked. At the first sign of aggression they turn the aggressor to toast.I've heard this movie called "fascist" because nobody wants a police force that has zero tolerance for aggression or anything else. But I'm not so sure. We have a police simulacrum built into our brains already. The brain is roughly dividable into three parts: (1) the reptilian brain that governs primitive functions like smelling and breathing, (2) the mammalian brain which generates and channels emotions like love and rage, and (3) the human brain, for lack of a better term, the huge cortex that sits on top of the others and damps down and regulates the more primitive emotions. If you remove the cortex in, say, a cat, what you get are digital emotions, either no anger at all or a full-blown rage.The problem with our built-in cop is that, like any other system of social control, it doesn't always work properly. When somebody insults us we hate him and want to punch him. That just "human nature", right? You can also call the impulse to do physical damage to another "pride" or "dignity" or "justifiable homicide" or "just desserts" -- but this is precisely the part of human nature that Tinker Toys like Gort will not tolerate.We remain free to kill those we hate. We can fly airplanes into their buildings or blow up their subways or invade their countries or fry them in an electric chair. We have the freedom to do that. But is this a kind of "freedom" we want to preserve? The freedom to express our most base impulses, the impulses that make us no different from monkeys or pit bulls or camels? Would we really lose that much if we turned ourselves over to the Gorts of the universe? If biology can't do the job can we turn it over to culture? If high schools can have zero tolerance policies for drug possession and if traffic cops can have zero tolerance for speeding, why not zero tolerance for killing? The Ten Commandments tells us clearly Thou Shallt Not Kill, without adding dozens of footnotes.The question is rhetorical. I don't know the answer. But the movie prompts us to ask it of ourselves, if we don't simply close down our logic when Klaatu starts going on about "police" and "absolute power" and "irrevocable." (An image forms out of Spiritus Mundi -- an Aryan in a Gestapo uniform.) Of course we hesitate. Anyone would. Klaatu has held back an important piece of information about Gort. Does he get free donuts?</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>George W. Bush and the Wisdom of Klaatu</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had never seen `Day the Earth Stood Still' until recently. I couldn't help but notice the uncanny resemblance Klaatu's ?with-us-or-against-us' view of Earth to President Bush's view of Iraq and his so-called Axis of Evil. Klaatu represents a planet who wants not to meddle in the affairs of Earth, but fears its experiments with atomic energy and its newfound ability to launch objects into space, could be merged-and pose a threat to other worlds (in this illustration we, as a planet, are Iraq). Its fears are well founded. The film is set in 1951, 6 years after the greatest war the world had ever seen. Looking at the world now, 52 years after `Day' came out, the outlook is, sadly, not much brighter. Bush's declaration to world leaders post-9/11 (a day the Earth really did come to a stop) on the ?war on terror,' `you are either with us or you are against us,' may not have been biblically-based (as it is believed to be). Maybe he'd just watched this film, near the end of which Klaatu makes an almost identical ultimatum to the people of the savage, divided people of this planet. Perhaps we can add 50s sci-fi to the possible reasons for Bush's misguided foreign policy on Iraq (and, hopefully not, North Korea). Interesting how `43' emulates Klaatu's words but not his philosophy-a policy of defense, of using force if threatened (we're talking real threats here, not manufactured ones). Still the message of `Day the Earth Stood Still' is a good one-we're all in this together. Back then, the message was supposed to be anti-nuclear. Watching it today, that doesn't come across as much as how we can't seem to get over our differences. The message still fits today because, as recent events have proven, we STILL can't all just get along. We can't even get everyone to agree on whom to attack. We're fighting over?whether to fight. Yes, the makers of `Day the Earth Stood Still' sure were on to something here. Klaatu saw how absurd our world is and will continue to be, hence the warning, ?change your course or be destroyed.'</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-09-25</date>
		<username>Cosmoeticadotcom (cosmoetica@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The other day I bought the DVD of one of my favorite all-time films: The Day The Earth Stood Still. In watching it I was struck by how relevant this sci fi classic from over 50 years ago is to today's political milieu. In brief, a flying saucer lands in Washington, D.C. Its occupant is a creature that looks human enough, named Klaatu (Michael Rennie). He is injured by fearful humans as the military surrounds the spacecraft. He is rescued by an all-powerful robot named Gort, who vaporizes the military machinery.Klaatu decides to infiltrate human society under the pseudonym Carpenter and takes up residence at a local boarding house, where he meets a widow named Helen Benson (Patricia Neal) who has a 10 year old son Bobby (Billy Gray of later Father Knows Best fame). In short, Klaatu is hunted down and killed by the military, then resurrected by Gort, who is aided by Helen, who knows the phrase that can control Gort: Klaatu Barada Nikto. Upon leaving Earth Klaatu issues an edict: either humans can change their ways or they will be exterminated if they spread their nuclear terror to the stars. Klaatu has only been an emissary from an organization called the United Planets. Gort is merely one of many all-powerful robots that police the UP registry and battle warring instinct with extermination- a policy all UP members submit to. With this knowledge Klaatu and Gort leave Earth to ponder its fate.Of course, the film has provoked many philosophical, ethical, and political salvos through the years. Made during the early years of the Cold War its symbolism was obvious, and its 'message' later became standard fare for much of the early 'real' UFO lore, depicted by the 'Contactees' of that era. But, its message still resonates today, in the post-Cold War restiveness of dying religious Fundamentalism's death throes. Especially since Rennie plays a near-Christ-like character.It has also been vociferously attacked as 'liberal' propaganda. Many Leftists of the day saw the film as a plea against the insanity of the nuclear arms race, which was insane- not because of the arms race mentality, but because total annihilation could occur by error, and not intent. Rightists pointed out that the 'peace' offered by Klaatu, Gort, and the UP comes only if humanity is either content to remain earthbound, or submit to the tyranny of the Gortian robocops. Counterarguments span range from the social compact gambit- i.e.- that while Earth is our home and we can do what we like the universal society at large has every right to set up its own laws and penalties. After all, we are free to parade around naked in our own homes, but not in public- to the sanity defense- that any society advanced enough to discover interstellar travel will likely create weaponry capable of planet destruction, therefore only a preemptive ban and enforcement can work.Yet, there does seem something amiss with Klaatu's UP. Klaatu shows he has the power to utterly immobilize human machinery (hence the film's title) yet it is oblivion or else- no degrees of shading, such as 'we'll take away all your nuclear toys if you humans are bad'. It's Armageddon or compliance. The UP has no problems with hypocrisy or 'benevolent' dictatorship. If we are too violent, by UP standards, they will counter with the ultimate violence. This is their law, and will be applied without any research into why humans fight: exploitation, racism, sociology, and ideological differences as reasons are all equally wrong, even if in self defense. The query rises- is peace without freedom a virtue?It has been counter-argued that there is such a thing as limited freedom- we do not have the right to infringe upon others' rights with our own. Klaatu claims the only freedom the UP has given up is the freedom of violence. Violence is the only freedom that is policed by Gort and his robotic brethren. The film has thus been seen as posing the question How would society function if disputes had to be resolved without violence? And it's a good query.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-04-24</date>
		<username>HelloTexas11</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>the day the earth got told off</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'The Day the Earth Stood Still' is one of the first, and best, sci-fi films to utilize a 'pompous alien' storyline. This has been a favorite device of science-fiction writers for as long as anyone can remember; it is practically a sub-genre and can be seen in productions as diverse as 'Plan Nine From Outer Space' and episodes of 'Star Trek: The Next Generation.' In these stories, a representative of a superior alien culture makes contact with we Earthlings, lectures us on our shortcomings, and usually threatens to destroy us unless we behave ourselves or kowtow to them or something. The enduring appeal of this type of sci-fi tale seems attributable to guilt feelings some have about mankind's failings, and also the recurring idea that when we finally meet visitors from another world, they will be better, smarter and more powerful than us. I suppose it would be a bit depressing to imagine meeting aliens for the first time and finding out they're a bunch of sniveling, worthless lowlifes. The high-minded alien in 'Day the Earth Stood Still' is Klaatu (Michael Rennie) who, along with his trusty sidekick Gort, gives Earth an ultimatum: behave yourselves or be destroyed. I don't know, for an advanced civilization that claims to worship peace above all else, that seems kind of harsh to me. What did we do to them? Did we go to THEIR planet and start bossing THEM around? But this is how the 'pompous alien' plot always goes. Anyway, Klaatu explains that the way his people have achieved peace is by creating a race of robots like Gort who are given absolute authority to stop any kind of hostile behavior. If only it were that simple. The movie makes one major change from the story it is based on; the Gort character (named Gnut, who can speak) corrects an Earth man who refers to Klaatu as Gnut's master. "You misunderstand," Gnut says. "I am the master." Which is a salient truth that would have to be the case for the system Klaatu describes to realistically function. The movie is well-produced with good performances by Rennie, Patricia Neal, and Hugh Marlowe. It is quite eerie at times; the great Bernard Herrmann contributes another of his classic music scores which helps immensely. It was a huge success and paved the way for all the great '50s sf/horror films that followed, from 'Forbidden Planet' to 'Invasion of the Body Snatchers.' And one can just see Ed Wood sitting in a dark theater watching it, absolutely mesmerized!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I've always loved this movie. It's from the days where the writing carried the movie instead of special effects. But I've never grasped why this movie is so loved by our friends of the left. Let's face it, the alien philosophy is conservatism to the core. They never cared what we did on our planet, to ourselves, it was our business and not theirs. But now we've reached the point where we might get off the planet, with nuclear weapons, and pose a threat to them. So they send an emissary, who's not nasty, or interested in conquering the Earth, with a simple message. The aliens demonstrate that they are immeasurably more powerful than we are, in a non-violent but still convincing manner, and then inform us, in simple terms, to cut it out and disarm. If we continue to pose a POTENTIAL future threat, they'll eliminate us now to avoid any possible trouble. That's it. No negotiation. Just their position, and fair warning. That's not a "peacenik" philosophy. The current remake is obviously aimed at proposing something more in line with our friends' views, as the aliens are out to punish us for not being "green" enough and being "nice" to the planet.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Intelligent sci-fi classic, but a little overrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have been hearing about "The Day The Earth Stood Still" since I was a little kid. It has become one of the most popular and best-loved sci-fi films ever. Now, having watched it, I can say that while it is a very good movie, it doesn't live up to the hype.The film stars Michael Rennie as the alien Klaatu, who comes to Earth with the message that its people must change our violent ways, or else our planet will be destroyed. He is shot as soon as he steps foot on the planet, and then treated badly by the government. He escapes from the hospital where he is imprisoned and befriends a widow (Patricia Neal) and her son (Billy Gray). Ignoring the hostility of Earth's people, Klaatu continuously tries to find a way to give us peace."The Day The Earth Stood Still" won a Golden Globe for "Best Film Promoting International Understanding". The greatest part of the film, and the biggest reason that it is respected, is that it only uses the aliens and science-fiction as the plot. The movie's message is about peace, and it states that message loud and clear throughout the film.The cast is great, especially Rennie as Klaatu - and who could forget Klaatu's assistant, the towering robot Gort, played by 7'7" Lock Martin? There's also a terrific score by Bernard Herrmann.This is a film that I would recommend to anyone. While it is, truly, a very good, intelligent sci-fi classic, I'd have to say it is a little overrated.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Blackboard Unfinished</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This old film is much admired. Most folks consider it science fiction and have it on their lists under that header; usually it is high on the list.My enthusiasm is often unbounded when it comes to projects like this, ones that worked in their time and were successful in the way intended. And I have great respect for the sort of world bending into alternatives that often uses "science fiction" devices. But I have to report that this film leaves me with less effect than any of a hundred monster and alien movies that had less purpose in mind.Its because the purpose is so vacuous. Was then, is now.The science fiction device ? basically just the appearance of a demigod ? is there to support the preaching of a simple message. Now I can tell you that in this era we all were terrified. Much of this was because there was a real threat, and much because ? as today ? the administration whipped up a narrative of impending, determined evil. Everyone wished for some higher level sense of reason and most believed they were part of it. This simply hooked into that desire. Its not a narrative, simply a small knothole into one that already existed.But there is something about the decisions they made. This film merely inserts a scifi plot device as I've noted. And the way they did this was to make a "real life" movie as much as possible. It seems that real Washington DC locations are used almost exclusively. The alien is as human as could be; likewise the lethal technology takes the form of a human.Even in small things, the way scenes are framed are all borrowed from small town movies though its set in a metropolis. We even have a family grouping that would be carried nearly intact into the Mayberry context, with Aunt Bee, an Opie, a girlfriend, even (in a predictable inversion) the professor transformed into the Don Knotts character.So there's some interesting stuff here in the decisions that filmmakers of ordinary skill made some simple decisions to enhance the effect of their film. But it isn't science fiction of any of the kinds I know. And it isn't an effective film in any way that matters.Ted's Evaluation -- 2 of 3: Has some interesting elements.</fullReview>

		<username>banderson-25</username>

		<shortDescription>great premise, horrible execution and ending</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>At the risk of sounding blasphemous, I just cant give this film my stamp of approval. There are so many things wrong with the story. I cant accept the idea that a flying saucer can land in the middle of Washington DC during the height of the Cold War, and the military response involves...cordoning off an area roughly fifty feet around the spaceship? A crowd of a thousand people are allowed to gawk in awe at a potentially hostile visitor, shoulder-to-shoulder with soldiers wielding automatic weapons (not to mention the tanks and heavy artillery). Klaatu, after being shot (an impossibly irresponsible act from a trained soldier), is whisked away to a hospital that seems content to simply lock the door to his room. That should hold this alien with the ability to travel at light speed and disintegrate army tanks! Later on, when Klaatu is gunned down a second time, Helen manages to elude the military and make it back to the spaceship to give Gort his orders. I could go on and on about the holes in the plot, but that isn't what really irritates me about this film. Plot holes can be forgiven. What cannot be forgiven, however, is Klaatu's notion that a Utopian society can be achieved through martial law. The only reason that Klaatu's people renounced violence was to avoid being zapped by Gort and his fellow robot police. Is this the way that humanity should pursue a world without violence? The constant threat of death? Robert Wise is an incredible director. His contributions cannot be underestimated. 		<shortDescription>Deeply Flawed, but Mostly Superb Science Fiction Classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Is "The Day the Earth Stood Still" one of the five best science fiction films of the fifties? Most definitely. Is "The Day the Earth Stood Still Still" one of the ten best science fiction films ever made? Arguably, though I think so. Is "The Day the Earth Stood Still" a classic science fiction film? Without a doubt? Is "The Day the Earth Stood Still" a masterpiece? Far from it. It's deeply flawed from a dramatic standpoint, almost crippled by illogical manipulations which advance the plot, while undermining its credibility. First, let's talk about what's excellent in "The Day ?" Bernard Hermann's score is not only wonderful, it's the best match of score and subject I've ever heard. Equally sublime is the photography. The special effects hold up extremely well, even after nearly 60 years. Robert Wise's direction is topnotch, as is Michael Rennie's performance as Klaatu. Gort, Klaatu's mute robot enforcer, is one of the most memorable character's in science fiction history.Now, let's talk about the movie's dramatic shortcomings:WARNING: SPOILERSKlaatu describes humans as frequently stupid. Well, Klaatu, how smart is it to:o Wear a helmet, disguising your humanity, in the midst of hundreds of nervous armedtroops and armored vehicles, when know one knows you are, what's your intent orwhat you look like? o Flash a device easily confused for a weapon, without first identifying it's purpose and taking even minimal precautions for your own protection? o Leave the protection of your ship when it is absolutely unnecessary, since your all important meeting with the world's scientists is going to take place right outside your ship?Certainly, the weakest plot device takes place when Klaatu is shot while he is trying to escape the army. Certainly the soldiers had strict orders NOT to shoot Klaatu without provocation; and, if Klaatu WAS shot under these circumstances, it would NOT be by a crew manning a jeep-mounted machine gun.Of course, this last plot device leads us to the most subversive element of "The Day ?" that being it's pseudo-religious implications. Clearly, Klaatu represents Jesus, while Gort represents the Torah's Jehovah. Klaatu MUST be killed, so he can be resurrected. Pretty heavy-handed stuff; but, fortunately, it works. So, unfortunately, does the misguided message that peace can been maintained by terror. Let's face it, that's what Klaatu and Gort are offering.END OF SPOILERSThis being said, "The Day the Earth Stood Still" is a fine movie, well worth seeing. I give "The Day the Earth Stood Still" an "8".</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>George W. Bush and the Wisdom of Klaatu</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I had never seen `Day the Earth Stood Still' until recently. I couldn't help but notice the uncanny resemblance Klaatu's ?with-us-or-against-us' view of Earth to President Bush's view of Iraq and his so-called Axis of Evil. Klaatu represents a planet who wants not to meddle in the affairs of Earth, but fears its experiments with atomic energy and its newfound ability to launch objects into space, could be merged-and pose a threat to other worlds (in this illustration we, as a planet, are Iraq). Its fears are well founded. The film is set in 1951, 6 years after the greatest war the world had ever seen. Looking at the world now, 52 years after `Day' came out, the outlook is, sadly, not much brighter. Bush's declaration to world leaders post-9/11 (a day the Earth really did come to a stop) on the ?war on terror,' `you are either with us or you are against us,' may not have been biblically-based (as it is believed to be). Maybe he'd just watched this film, near the end of which Klaatu makes an almost identical ultimatum to the people of the savage, divided people of this planet. Perhaps we can add 50s sci-fi to the possible reasons for Bush's misguided foreign policy on Iraq (and, hopefully not, North Korea). Interesting how `43' emulates Klaatu's words but not his philosophy-a policy of defense, of using force if threatened (we're talking real threats here, not manufactured ones). Still the message of `Day the Earth Stood Still' is a good one-we're all in this together. Back then, the message was supposed to be anti-nuclear. Watching it today, that doesn't come across as much as how we can't seem to get over our differences. The message still fits today because, as recent events have proven, we STILL can't all just get along. We can't even get everyone to agree on whom to attack. We're fighting over?whether to fight. Yes, the makers of `Day the Earth Stood Still' sure were on to something here. Klaatu saw how absurd our world is and will continue to be, hence the warning, ?change your course or be destroyed.'</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Movies are supposed to transport us to other worlds; worlds based on *how* things are seen, not so much 'where' or 'what happens'. It's the 'how' that draws us in, regardless of topic.Cinematically, this is completely pedestrian. The acting and camera work are akin, in that they are equally stiff, flat and characterless. The 'how' never gets off the ground. It took enormous film-making capability to overcome these problems during this period. Transcendent examples include "Rear Window".But a few things help prop it up. It's rooted in a remarkably simple idea that taps into much bigger notions shared with the audience. With Capra, we had pure jingoism. Here we have equal opportunity obliteration. Science is the ultimate medium of equal opportunities; it's no wonder that the narrative is placed at this level: 'matter of fact'.The cold calculation forms the necessary platform for the visual retelling of "War of the Worlds" two years later. It is also a distant forerunner of the "Terminator" series.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The original sci-fi 'classic'.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Day the Earth Stood Still starts in Washington D.C. as an alien spacecraft lands in a baseball park, the army is called in &amp; soon the spacecraft is surrounded. An alien man named Klaatu (Michael Rennie) &amp; a tall robot named Gort (Lock Martin) emerge from the craft, Klaatu is about to hand over a gift for the President when one soldier who believes it to be a weapon opens fire &amp; shoots Klaatu, in retaliation Gort destroys all the guns &amp; tanks surrounding the craft. Klaatu is taken to hospital &amp; makes an amazing recovery, he asks to speak to all the worlds leaders but the level of suspicion makes this request impossible. Klaatu escapes from his hospital room &amp; checks into a boarding house in order to observe human nature better &amp; set his plans in motion to speak to a collection of Earth's scientists, Klaatu warns that unless humanity stops the nuclear arms race his people will be forced to destroy Earth &amp; everything living creature on it...Directed by Robert Wise this was based on the short story 'Farewell to the Master' by Harry Bates who was apparently paid just $500 for it, widely considered as one of the true greats of the sci-fi genre I have to say that while The Day the Earth Stood Still is a good film I would be very hard pressed to call it a great one let alone a genre defining classic. The script is sci-fi with a message, it's a message &amp; a warning about nuclear armament &amp; that we heading for total destruction if we carry on. It's a point quite well made even if it's been done to death over the years so maybe doesn't feel anywhere near as original as it once did. However as a film I found it rather dated &amp; the script quite creaky, your telling me that when an alien lands on Earth &amp; end sup in the hospital he will be unguarded in his room &amp; free to escape? The authorities have no pictures or photo's of him at all? The spacecraft gets a paltry two guards? People are allowed to stand mere feet from the spacecraft when it first arrives? You need to suspend your disbelief quite a lot while watching this, the script really isn't that good &amp; apart from a strong central message &amp; one or two striking moments I can't say I liked The Day the Earth Stood Still that much, sure it's a good solid film worth a watch but I don't think it's the undisputed classic that a lot of people &amp; so-called experts consider it to be. After a great opening in which the spaceship lands &amp; Klaatu emerges the script settles down into a human interest story as the human looking alien Klaatu observes people &amp; tries to make his own mind up if we are worth saving before one final message at the end.Certainly well made with special effects that hold up quite well even today, at least there are no tentacled aliens or monsters &amp; Gort the robot who was played by 7'7 Lock Martin is memorable even if the crinkles &amp; creases in his suit when he moves lessens the effect. Well shot with good use of shadow too. Composer Bernard Hermann provides a good score as well.With a supposed budget of about $1,200,000 this has good production values &amp; is well made, although set mainly in Washington this was actually mainly filmed in Los Angeles. The acting is pretty good, nothing amazing but decent enough.The Day the Earth Stood Still is regarded by many as an absolute classic but not by me, I don't think time has been that kind to it &amp; with a fairly serious &amp; sombre message it's not the most fun film to just sit down &amp; watch. Remade by Hollywood as The Day the Earth Stood Still (2008).</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>suitable for children at best.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's important to keep in mind that the story this film is based on, like much other "Golden Age" science fiction, is deservedly forgotten. What survives from that era of science fiction are a few names--Clarke, Asimov, Heinlein--and a slew of stories by other authors that can genuinely be appreciated for their vision and storytelling. "The Day The Earth Stood Still" however seems more rooted in the insipid science fiction of the era, the type that was guaranteed to be forgotten fairly soon. Everything about this film is banal and hokey--surely gripping at the time, but quite silly and laughable by today's standards. It isn't even on par with the best episodes of The Twilight Zone or The Outer Limits, and the message strikes one as obvious and heavy handed. It's a wonder how adults can continue to find merit in a film with such a puerile center.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Day the Earth Turned Silly.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>And I thought the movie itself was stupid! Little did I know how snicker-worthy it would be to read that this laughable little 50s piece of sci-fi cheese is on IMDb's top 250 list. No doubt a bunch of clueless and confused film students had something to do with that...A silly-looking flying saucer (no, not a UFO; UFOs are "unidentified objects", remember) lands in Washington D.C., and who exits the elegant-looking vehicle? A suave-looking human with finely cut hair and impeccable gentleman's-club manners. (I guess this was one of the moments that really had those film students relieving themselves in their movie diapers.) Earth people, who are all portrayed as utter idiots, send a military squadron with orders to shoot at will. All these highly-trained soldiers are on the verge of a nervous breakdown - that's how excited and scared they get that a human dressed as a gay diver approaches them. I found it infinitely amusing that the military would be sweating bullets about this alien, while the civilian spectators would just casually grin and not show any signs of distress at all! Of course the alien, whose name is Klutz, is so completely above us all in intelligence, sophistication, morals, and knowledge - and yet the doofus fails to predict that a violent species such as Earthlings would misread his action of pulling out an object that looks like a weapon. Klutz gets shot. At that moment, I could just hear him saying "See? See?? Your stupid minds! Stupid! Stupid!". Klutz doesn't exactly scream "stupid", but he does sound very hurt, bitching like a little baby that the toy in his hands was not a weapon but a gift for mankind. If Klutz is so totally All-Knowing, then why did he not inform the masses of people that had gathered there that he was about to take out a mere movie-prop toy? Perhaps Klutz wanted to get shot, so he'd become an intergalactic martyr? Or maybe he just didn't do his homework and prepare for his mission with the degree of seriousness that this requires. Or maybe Klutz is just plain DUMB.Klutz informs us that his toy is a gift "to study life on other planets". Hey, Klutzy, how about getting us a SPACESHIP first! It's like giving someone a guitar pick for birthday instead of a guitar. What exactly does this little device do? Detect moronic scripts on other planets? Foolish alien film students? 1950s actors that always just stand around motionless like statuettes during the dialogues? Klutz can do almost anything throughout the movie, even magic, but he can't set up a pair of speakers to address all of the world's leaders. "Yeah, I've come from a long way away, in a very advanced spacecraft, we've beaten death and disease, and we have robots that can do just about everything except sing YESTERDAY in baritone, but our civilization knows nothing about sound transmission. Please help, little freckled boy!" What is with these 50s sci-fi flicks and their Irish-immigrant pre-school boys who befriend the oh-so misunderstood, noble aliens? Isn't that a bit of a hint whom these films were primarily targeted at? Kids and morons.I loved the part when Klutz tells one of the adults (yes, he occasionally communicated with adults - when he wasn't too busy wasting his precious mission time with little boys) that the nuclear missiles that mankind had developed were a threat to the other planets. That was a good one... But, hey... Hang on! If the Intergalactic United Nations are so concerned about this threat then WHY go to Earth hence reveal themselves, their existence, location etc? "We know you are extremely dangerous to us, what with your short-range missiles that can't even hit the Moon, but we want to tell you where WE are, so that if you ever DO feel like obliterating something outside your own orbit you can have a proper target!" Klutz's visit is about 10,000 years too early: for the life of me I can't imagine that humans could launch an unstoppable nuclear projectile toward the closest planet in the next couple of aeons. Why would these silly Martians, Venusians (and other B-movie "ians") worry about this utterly hypothetical threat in the first place? They should be so advanced that shooting down a potential missile coming from primitive little Earth should be child's play. But I forget that an organization that sends a clod like Klutz to Earth can't be a particularly smart organization...Between the fairly eventful beginning and the very Grand Finale there is almost nothing going on in the film; just a lot of padding: Klutz in the stereotypical "stranded and somewhat confused alien" role, plus the half-hearted establishing of some rather unessential and dull human characters. Will she marry him? Won't she? That is certainly the Question of the Day when an alien spaceship sits not far from the White House...What silly 50s kids' movie would be complete without a robot or a monster carrying a damsel-in-distress... The damsel (Patricia Neal) isn't particularly attractive. (No wonder her fiancé treats her badly.) Why can't these B-movie damsels walk on their own? Why do they faints so easily? These are the urgent questions the movie failed to answer. Instead, we get an idealistic, Hollywood-type speech by Klutz that finally clarifies a few things. Of course, this alien moron could have said all of that on the day that he landed, hence saving everyone the trouble of watching all those dull goings-on in-between.If this "race of robots" prevents all violence between the aliens, does that mean that there are security cameras everywhere, including toilet facilities? If so, I should think that losing one's bathroom privacy would be too high a price to pay, even for peace...Go to http://rateyourmusic.com/~Fedor8 and check out my "TV &amp; Cinema: 150 Worst Cases Of Nepotism" list.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A deliciously silly film for the perennially young at heart</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Most of my friends and I - we would go together to see films such as "The Day The Earth Stood Still" in packs of 8 or more - thought it was, well, sort of silly. (This was in 1951, we were 16 or so and too sophisticated to be impressed by flying saucers that looked something like pie pans.) And to us Michael Rennie appeared to be awfully wooden; he reminded us of our math teacher. And Gort - his shiny metal, indestructible gofer - wasn't a very interesting-looking alien; he bore a striking resemblance to Jack Haley as the Tin Man in "The Wizard of Oz." (Of course this was eons before the likes of R2D2 and "2001 Space Odessy.") But the thing that is most delightful about the film is that the magic MumboJumbo phrase, "Klaatu Barrada Nikto," which I believe Rennie (who played Klaatu) told Patricia Neal to memorize and say to Gort when she approached Gort so that he wouldn't zap her or eat her for breakfast - had that certain . . . something such that it became part of the lexicon of our generation; when my mother would yell for me in the middle of a ball game to come in for dinner I would pause, pull myself up to my full height, and haughtily announce to her, "Klaatu Barrada Nikto" - and then run from the broom that she invariably tried to swat me with. And amazingly the apparent deliciousness of the mystique of that phrase has survived to this day. No less a distinguished columnist than Maureen Dowd used it in a syndicated New York Times column on August 17, 2003, to segue into a discussion of a power outage that the East Coast was experiencing at the time. (In the film Klaatu demonstrates his powers by causing a world-wide "freeze" of electricity for 30 minutes or so, stopping everything mechanical from moving, in order to prove to humans that he wasn't fooling around!) So those ridiculous three words came to be part of the wonderful magic of Hollywood; once in a while some trifle catches on, is loved by movie fans everywhere, and survives. Klaatu Barrada Nikto.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'The Day the Earth Stood Still' applies to our day in age much better than many other black and white movies do. The movie landscapes different views of peace and how it is accomplished, various nations' inability to cooperate with each other, and also serves as an entertaining Sci-Fi film for the whole family. You just can't beat an entertaining, educational movie. Similar movies that come to mind are Titanic, Good Will Hunting, and Black Hawk Down. Although these movies are completely different, they each portray a message or a story line that can instigate a healthy class discussion. I particularly enjoyed the way some people reacted to Klaatu. Some, like Professor Barnhardt, were fascinated by Klaatu's intelligence and way of life. They embraced his advice and advocated his idea of peace. Others wanted nothing more than to throw him in jail. The balance between who wished to get to know Klaatu and who wanted to kill him was actually very different than how it would be if a real alien landed on earth. People would be heading for the hills. And if he implied that our time on Earth was limited, America would start planning ways to attack him or his planet. Everyone would react violently as opposed to just crumbling beneath his orders. Something that I found worthy of note was the fact that the movie ended before a solution was reached. They decided to leave that part up to the audience. This enables people to come up with their own resolutions for a problem that we face today. When nothing is at stake, and all that might be sacrificed are some people on the big screen, the problem seems much simpler and easier to resolve. Therefore, an answer is much closer to us.Some things I didn't like as much were the space suits that Klaatu and Gort wear throughout the movie. I've been spoiled by high tech costumes and amazing special effects in movies that appear in theaters today. The lack of pizzazz among the costumes, sets, and story plot are a breath of fresh air because the element of surprise is left to the music, acting, and lighting. Still, all in all, the movie was enjoyable and I recommend it to anyone with a free Saturday night.</fullReview>

That and a few other things got a few laughs out of me, such as all technology stalled, but Helen's watch kept ticking (and the government was given reports from all around the world, and the world seemed to be at daylight all at the same time, etc) and the baseball field the ship landed in suddenly grew trees when both Klaatu and Bobby ventured to at night ? at least they could hide. Though the logic (yes, I know it's about a space man &amp; robot) was out the window, the heart's definitely apparent in this feature. I also loved the acting of the leading lady Helen and even Klaatu ? he shares my opinion of the idiots we call humans, or more so, leaders. Sad to see how childish we humans (or, again, leaders) are portrayed in the film, and even though this came out in 1951, I imagine the same would happen today ? or worse. Granted, I didn't see the film when it first landed in theatres (born in 1974), nor did I see it until tonight for the first time; I still have seen greater "classics" of that era. I had no desire to see the updated version coming out December 2008, specifically due to Keanu Reeves, but I figured if I did see it, I might as well watch the original. So far, I see only two similarities from the original and the trailers ? the title and it's about a man from outer space ? that's about it. Strange, I would've thought Keanu would've played the robot Gort vs Klaatu due to his normal acting range.</fullReview>
